ON THE DEATH OF GENERAL GEORGE
WASHINGTON.

BY A YOUNG LADY.

What means the solemn dirge that strikes my ear?
What means those mournful sounds—why shines
	the tear?
Why toll the bells the awful knell of fate?
Ah! why those sights that do my fancy sate?

Where’er I turn the general gloom appears,
Those mourning badges fill my soul with fears;
Hark! yonder rueful noise! ’tis done!
The silent tomb invades our Washington!

Must virtues exalted, yield their breath?
Must bright perfection find relief in death?
Must mortal greatness fall? A glorius name!
What then is riches, honor and true fame?

The august chief, the father and the friend,
The generous patriot-let the muse commend;
Columbia’s glory, and Mount Vernon’s pride.
There lies enshrined with numbers at his side!

There let the sigh respondent from the breast,
Heave in rich numbers! let the glowing zest;
Of tears refulgent beam with grateful love;
And the sable mourning our affliction prove.

Weep! kindred mortals—weep! no more you’ll find,
A man so just, so pure, so firm in mind;
Rejoicing angels, hail the he venly sage!
Celestial spirits greet the wonder of the Age!
	January, 1800.                       Ulster Co. Gazette.


[Column 1]
SKETCH OF THE LIFE OF MRS. ELIZABETH
M. HART.

Editor Exponent.
	The following brief sketch of the life of
Mrs. Elizabeth M. Hart was written by
herself during the first week in January,
1904, and read by her in the Young Ladies’
Mutual Improvement Association in Bloom-
ington on Sunday evening, Jan. 10th. 1904,
five days previous to the deplorable acci-
dent which caused her death.
	She was a domestic economist in the ful-
lest sense of the word; a wise counselor, a
well read and thoughtful student; a ready
and interesting public speaker and a sin-
cere and devout member of the Church of
Jesus Christ, and made loving friends
wherever she was known.
	It is the consensus of the feelings of the
Stake Presidency and the auxiliary organi-
zations of which deceased was an active
member for so many years that in her de-
parture they have lost one of the bright and
shining lights among the noble women who
labor among us so faithfully in building up

[Column 2]
the great work of the Lord in this Stake of
Zion.                                     COM.
February 10th, 1904.

	I was born on the 26th day of March in
the year 1843, in the town of Treforest, in
the county of Glamorgan, South Wales,
Great Britain. My father’s name was David
Thomas, my mother Martha Davis Thomas,
both of the same place. I am the second
daughter of a family of six girls. Our par-
ents were in comiortable circumstances, and
we grew up in an atmosphere of love and
tenderness. Our home was to me a perfect
paradise. Although my mother’s health
was delicate, she was able to direct home
affairs, but obliged to leave the arduous
labors to an attendant named “Kitty,” who
lived in the family seventeen years.
	My father did not believe in any of the
religions of the so-called Christian churches;
but he was a strong believer and advocate
of justice, truth and equity. My mother
was a member of the Baptist Church, but
was not satisfied with its organizations and
doctrines, and was always searching for
more light, and something more tangible
that she could lay hold on with full hope
and assurance. She was fervent in spirit,
conscientious in her convictions, and faith-
ful in performing her duties in life as she
understood them.
	From my earliest recollection I was
taught to observe the Sabbath day to keep
it holy; to refrain from loud laughter and
from indulging in mirth of whatever nature.
I was fairly well acquainted with the
common branches of education, including
domestic economy, before I reached my
thirteenth year.
	In the year 1855 the family moved to
Cardiff, a large sea-port town, in South
Wales, where my father was offered a more
remunerative position as a pattern maker, in
a large steel rail establishment. We were
then about twelve miles from the home of
my maternal grandmother, Mrs. Davis. I
can now picture in my mind the thatch
roofed farm house, with my dear old grand-
mother bustling around, going five miles to
meeting and willing to indulge me in every
way.
	In 1856 we were called to mourn the loss
of our baby sister, from cholera infantum.
My mother’s health was quite frail at this
time, and required the tenderest care and
attention from my sister and myself, and
although our change of residence did not
prove as successful in a pecuniary sense as
we expected, my mother felt confident that
the Lord was mindful of her pleadings. It
so happened that a year had scarcely elap-
sed when the light of the Gospel was
brought to her, by a good old lady who
came to do our washing. My mother
accepted it without any question or hesita-
tion, and it seemed to inspire new life in
her, so much so, that within a month she
was baptized and confirmed a member of
the Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day
Saints.
	At my mother’s urgent request my eldest
sister and myself also, became members of
the Church. I yielded obedience to the

[Column 3]
Gospel from a desire to gratify my mother’s
wishes; for up to that time I had not given
much thought or reflection to matters of
religion, I was keenly sensitive and consci-
ous of right and wrong, but it seemed to
me sufficient, if a person lived up to the
golden rule, “do unto others as you would
have them do unto you.”
	In 1856 my father was baptized also and
became a member of the Church. He met
with great opposition from his relatives and
former friends, and up to this day my sister
Mrs. Samuel H. Hill and myself are the
only representatives of my father’s house in
the Church.
	In March, 1857, we commenced our
travels to the land of Zion. We sailed on
the “George Washington” from Liverpool 
and landed in Boston on the 17th, of April,
having been three weeks on the ocean. We
encountered some very severe gales, but all
things considered we had a fair and pleas-
ant journey as intermediate passengers. I
shall however, never forget the disagreeable
sensation of sea-sickness experienced upon
the voyage.
	We arrived in Iowa City, which was then
the terminus of the railroad, a week later.
The Saints were going from that point by
teams and hand carts. Instead of continu- 
ing our journey as at first intended, much
to my mother’s disappointment, my father
decided to remain there another year, so
that we could go with more comfort and in-
dependence. We did not however realize
our expectations; reverses came upon us.
There was no work my father could engage
in. My father and mother were both taken
down with ague and fever and were unable
to help each other or themselves for nearly
a year. Expenses accumulated so that we
had to dispose of our chinaware, cutlery,
and many other articles that we had pro-
vided for our use and comfort. It was then
that life’s stern realities dawned upon me
as never before.
	It was about this time that Governor
Kirkwood of Iowa and his wife engaged
me as second girl at fair wages. Although
they were kind and considerate, I realized
my position keenly as a subordinate and
subject to their bidding, which at first, was
quite a trial to me.
	I had many experiences of this kind but
the thought that I was thus able to assist
my parents in the expenses of home, gave
me courage, and made me more brave than
if I were the only one benefited by my labor.
	Then came an agreeable change; my
father regained his health and took a con-
tract to build Governor Kirkwood a frame
house which, when completed, netted us
quite a sum and enabled us to meet our
financial obligations. It was then decided
that I go to school, leaving the girls at
home with mother, while I worked for my
board with a Mrs. Dunlap whose home was
a mile nearer the school.
	It was there I met Mr. George M. Keen,
a cousin of Mr. Dunlap, who had come on a
visit from St. Paul, Minn. He was a
scholar and by profession a journalist.
When he left for his home a month later,
it was with the promise that we open up a


[Column One]
friendly correspondence. This was in July, 
1860, and on the 21st, of April, 1861, we
were married. My mother was very much
opposed to our marriage because he was
not of our faith and she desired to take all
of her children to Utah. It was in the same
month and year the civil war began, and
when the call was made for seventy-five
thousand volunteers, Mr. Keen could not
resist that spirit of patriotism that fills the
heart of every native born American when
his country is in jeopardy. He joined the
Second Iowa Cavalry, and was engaged in
active service in many battles, but escaped
unhurt, to return home and resume the
avocations of life. Eleven months after his
return he contracted a severe cold, which
developed into pneumonia and on the 6th, 
of September, 1865, he passed away.
	In the mean time, that is in the year
1862, during Mr. Keen’s service in the
Union army, I moved to Chicago with my
father and mother and spent part of the time
in Salem, Ohio, with Mr. Keen’s parents
who did all they could to make life pleasant.
	In 1868, I came with my parents to Salt
Lake City. We located in the 9th Ward,
where we were re-baptized and were re-
ceived as members of the Church. We sub-
sequently moved to the central part of the
city, where to meet the necessary expenses
we took in boarders. My father being un-
able to obtain employment as a machinist, 
became discouraged and had gone back to
Chicago, expecting my mother would re-
turn also, if left to depend upon her own
resources; but with the help of her girls and
the blessings of the Lord, we managed to
live and pay expenses. 
	House rents and provisions were then so
high that we could not afford to hire any-
thing done, and to keep the class of board-
ers we had, required unceasing labor; in
fact it was what my be called a slave’s life.
Notwithstanding our board rates were two
dollars per day, we could only pay the
rent and keep the table supplied. 
	Our relatives in Provo were constantly
urging us to go there and make our home
and upon my mother receiving a check
from my father for a liberal sum, my
mother decided to move to Provo, and
within a month we had bought a lot there
with one large room upon it, making it our
home, with an addition of a shed at the
back. We stored our furniture away until
we could make further building improve-
ments. 
	I am thankful that I had such a faithful
mother, for it was through her faith and
untiring efforts and loyalty to her religion, 
that I am indebted for being brought to
these mountains and for a name and stand-
ing in the Church of Jesus Christ in these
last days.
	We soon became identified with one of
the ward organizations and I was given a 
class in the Sunday School. It was there
in Provo I became acquainted with Sister
Emily Hart, who was a warm friend of my
dear mother. I was introduced by her to
Elder James H. Hart, to whom I was mar-
ried in 1873, with her full, and free consent
and approval, also that of my dear mother,
who believed in, and fully endorsed the
principle of plural marriage.
	In 1874 I moved to Bloomington, Bear
Lake County, Idaho, with my husband. I
was soon identified with the Bloomington
Sunday School and with the ward Primary
Association of which I was chosen and ap-
pointed President in the year 1879. I was
also a member of the Young Ladies’ Mutual

[Column Two]
Improvement Association at the same time, 
and was appointed Stake Secretary of that
organization the same year. I took great
pleasure and satisfaction in traveling with
the President, Mrs Nancy E. Pugmire and
her counselors in all parts of the Bear Lake
Stake of Zion and was honorably released
in September, 1903, covering a period of
twenty-four years.
	In the year 1884 I was chosen and set
apart as a counselor in the Stake Presidency 
of the Relief Society of this Stake of Zion, 
a position I still hold, being associated with
sister Julia Lindsay as her first counselor
and ably assisted by Mary J. Bagley as sec-
ond counselor.
	In conclusion I can say that in the labor
herein mentioned in which we have traveled
many thousands of miles, not in Bear Lake
County only, but in Rich County, Utah,
and in Star Valley, Uinta County, Wyom-
ing, formerly belonging to this Stake organ-
ization during all seasons of the year, and
sometimes in the most inclement weather,
that in these labors and associations among
the daughters of Zion and the noble moth-
ers in Israel, they have afforded the great-
est happiness that I have experiened during
my life. 
			ELIZABETH M. HART.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT


[Column 1]
MOTHERS WORK.
TAYLOR STAKE.

I.—Woman, her Sphere and Mission.

	She openeth her mouth with wisdom and
in her tongue is the law of kindness. Wo-
man that feareth the Lord shall be blessed.
Prov. 31. God’s commandment to Adam
and Eve (Gen. 1:28 Gen. 3;16).

II.—Children are a gift from God. Doc.
	and Cov. Sec. 132.

	Care of parents in setting proper ex-
amples before their children. Instiling
faith in the hearts of their children.

III.—Virtue and Moral purity.

	A child most be taught purity from its
infancy. Pure thoughts make a happy
mind. Let your thoughts be chaste and
pure. Evil thoughts make vice and crime;
honest thoughts and noble deeds build a
character sublime.

IV.—Scolding and Threatening.

	Scolding and threatening are real sins,
govern by love and kindness. “Kind words
are bright flowers of earth’s existence.”

V.—Finding fault with our children.

	It is at times necessary to censure and
punish, but very much more may be done
by encouraging children when they do well.
Nothing can more discourage a child than
incessant fault finding on the part of its
parents.

VI.—Use of Drugs and Patent Medicine.

	Discourage the use of any deadening med-
icine to be given to our children. Encourage
faith in God and the ordinances of the
Gospel. Doc. and Cov. sec. 42.

VII.—Artificial feeding of Infants how
	prepared.

VIII.—Diet and Dressing of Children.

	How often, we notice mothers who with

[Column 2]
great earnestness study the cultivation and
treatment of flowers to make them healthy,
and beautiful, and neglect their children,
how to take proper care of those noble
spirits whom the Lord has entrusted to our
care that their little bodies may be healthy
and strong.

IX.—Moral Training of Children.

	It is a mistaken idea mothers that infants
are not capable of being trained in any
way until they are several months or a year
old.

X.—Care of Amusements for the Young.

	Evil of card playing amoung our people.
Juvenile Instructor Aug. and Sept. No’s by
Pres. Joseph F. Smith.

XI.—Order of Marriage.

	First step commence aright, may save
unhappiness. Doc. and Cov. Sec. 132.

XII.—Object of Relief Society Work.

	Also the object of our Mothers Work.
Ref. the Prophet Joseph Smith at the first
Relief Society meeting.
			HANNAH M. RUSSELL,
			ROZILLA GRANT,
			ELIZABETH HARDY.

		PANGUITCH STAKE.

Lesson I.—Woman.

	‘Oh, woman, woman, know thyself,
	And know thy mission too.
	Thou comest from thy makers hands,
	A mighty work to do.”
						—Eliza R. Snow.

				Woman.
	A. What is she?
		1. Child of God.
		2. Companion of her husband.
	B. Her mission.
		1. Child. Duties of child to parents.
		2. Sister. Duties of a sister to house-
		hold and to mankind.
		3. Wife. “Nevertheless, neither is
		the man without the woman,
		neither the woman without the
		man in the Lord” (I Cor. 11: 11).
		4. Mother. Duty to become a
		mother. “Be fruitful, and multi-
		ply and replenish the earth”
		(Gen. 1: 28).
	C. Her Reward.
		1. Earthly possessions. Husband,
		children. Consciousness of hav-
		ing obeyed commandments of
		God.
		2. Heavenly possessions. Celestial
		inheritance.

Lesson II.—Motherhood.

“O wondrous power! how little understood;
Entrusted to the mother’s mind alone,
To fashion genius, form the mind for good.

	A. It is a wife’s duty to become a
	mother.
	B. Pre-natal influence.
		1. Habits of mother. Like begets
		like.
	C. Preparation for confinement.
		1. Diet.
		2. Clothing.


[Column 1]
Lesson III.—The Baby.
	“I love these little people, and it is not a
a slight thing when they, who are so fresh
from God love us."			Dickens.
	The Baby.
	A. Bath.
		I. Time to bathe. Regularity. Why
		not in health only but in sickness,
		also. Daily bath.
		2. How to bathe. First bath of oil.
		Following every bath with a
		massage of oil. Preparations for
		the bath. How to proceed.
	B. Clothing.
		I. Hygienic dress. How made.
		Loose, light, free from pins. Not
		too long. Why? Bring samples.
		Change of clothing. Material.
		Woolen. Cotton. Which? Why?
	C. Food.
		I. System in feeding. In food and
		drink. Either natural or artificial
		food.
Lesson IV.—Children and their Diseases.
	A. Colic.
		I. Symptoms.
		2. Treatment. Thoroughly warm.
		Apply hot flannels. Rub body
		with oil.
	B. Thrush. Tell cause and how cured.
	C. Diarrhoea.
		I. Cause.
		2. Treatment.
	D. Spasmodic croup.
		I. Cause. Exposure to damp and
		cold, excitement and indigestion.
		2. Cure. Apply warm flannel to
		throat. Give mustard bath or
		foot bath an emetic and a simple
		enema.
		3. How to prevent a return.
Lesson V. —Home.
	“Home’s not merely roof and room
	It needs something to endear it
	Home is where the heart can bloom,
	Where there’s some kind lip to cheer it.”
				Home.
	A. Cleanliness.
		I. Of house and yards. Sanitary and
		moral effects. Why.
		2. Of carpets, floors, walls, nooks
		and corners, bedding, clothing
		and food.
	B. Regularity.
		I. Work.
		2. Study.
		3. Devotional exercises.
		4. Shopping.
	C. Manners at home.
		I. Politeness. The golden rule is the
		embodiment of all true politeness.
		2. Personal habits. Cleanliness of
		person. “A clean mind can not
		dwell in an unclean body.”
Lesson VI.—Home (continued).
	A great many homes are like the frame of
a harp that stands without strings. In form
and outline they suggest music; but no
melody rises from the empty spaces; and
thus it happens that home is unattractive,
dreary and dull.

[Column 2]
A. Amusements.
	I. Books. A library is not a luxury
	but one of the necessaries of life.
	Books of information are the tools
	for life’s daily use. Books of in-
	spiration are the instruments of
	music for life’s loftiest and most
	sacred moments.
	2. Music. Music is not a mere orna-
	ment it is an educative power.
	How? What is its moral effect?
	How does it bring about har-
	mony?
	3. Games. Educational. Avoid
	games that border on familiarity.
	Give substance of Presidents
	Smith’s talk on card playing.
B. Ethics.
	1. Pleasant and attractive surround-
	ings. It is as much the duty of
	parents to beautify their home
	as it is to keep the moral atmos-
	phere of that home pure.
	2. Agreeable associates.
	3. Parents love for child. No person
	has the right to be the parent of
	a child who does not love him.
	4. Obligations of prrents and chil-
	dren are reciprocal. Why? A
	smile is the most useful thing in
	the world iu proportion to its cost.
			JANE LEFEVRE,
			MAMIE F. DODDS,
			KATE G. CLARK,
					Committee.

Lesson VIII.—Results of Disobedience.
	(Ref. Mosiah 11th and 12th Chapters).
	We cannot plan and build for ourselves, 
if we do we will be like the Jews of Old, as
the Prophet says: “Have hewn out cisterns,
that will hold no water.” We must submit
to the ordinances of the House of the Lord.
Lesson IX.—Home.
	Study to meet all your requirements as a
wife in righteousness. Give personal ex-
perience as “The Mother of the Home.”
The mother and daughters [-]eed to realize
that woman’s life and liberty does not con-
sist in the multitude of avenues that lead
from home to the outside world; but in
the uumber of avenues from which she may
bring the best, from the outside world, into
the home.
Lesson X.—Duties of Parents to Children.
	Parents be just to your, children in the
least, as well as in the greatest thing. The
home without children is lonely, and want-
ing in the love element. Every child born
into this world is entitled to the best rear-
ing.
Lasson XI.—Amusements for the Home.
	The woman who takes into her heart, her
own children, may be a very ordinary 
woman, but the woman who takes into her
heart, the children of others, is one of God’s
Mothers.” It is natural that we should
love our children, but every child in the 
community is ours, in so far as our influence
makes him a better or worse citizen.
Lesson XII.—Review of the Year's Work.
				ELIZA MUIR ORAM,
				ELVIRA C. STEELE,
				ELIZABETH S. BRUNDT,
				JENSINE JOHANNESEN,
				ALICE BOOMER,
						Committee.
		TILLIE E. WRIGHT,
					Secretary.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


LADY HENRY SOMERSET contributes to 
the December Cosmopolitan an interesting
article on “British Social Life.” She is
herself a prominent member of the old
English aristocracy, and her article on the
new fashionable London of to day is at
once a commentary on the tendency of the
times and a protest against some of its
harmful features.

SALT LAKE CITY, UTAH, FEBRUARY, 1904


RELIEF SOCIETY CONFERENCE.
	The General Annual Conference of the
Relief Society will be held in the Salt Lake
Assembly Hall in this city, Saturday, April
2, 1904, meetings commencing at 10 a. m.
and 2 p. m. It is expected that each Stake
organization of the Relief Society will be
represented by the president or an author-
ized representative. All members of the
society are cordially invited to attend the
conference. Bishops and brethren interested
in the charitable work of the sisters will
be most welcome, also officers and mem-
bers of Y. L. N. M. I. A., and Primary
Associations.
		BATHSHEBA W. SMITH,
						President.
		ANNIE TAYLOR HYDE,
		IDA S. DUSSENBERRY,
						Counselors.


EDITORIAL NOTES.
	There are several Stake orgonizations of
the Relief Society that have not yet sent in
their annual reports for the year 1903.
Please take notice, as the time is near at
hand for the general report to be made to
the Conference, Please address Mrs. E. B.
Wells. General Secretary, Templeton Build-
ing, Salt Lake City.
	The National Woman’s Suffrage Associa-
tion, which met recently in Washington,
D C., elected Rev. Anna H. Shaw, president,
Mrs. Carrie Chapman Catt, having refused
to be a candidate for re-election. The Inter-
national Woman’s Suffrage Conference will
be held in Berlin, probably June 20 and
21. Each of the ten countries affiliated will
be entitled to six delegates and six alter-
nates.


D. R. STATE SOCIETY
	The daughters of the Revolution held the
regular monthly meeting Tuesday, Feb. 16,
1904, at the home of Mrs. Clarissa S. Wil-
liams. The handsome parlors were deco-
rated with the stars and stripes and other
effective emblems, pictures, flowers, etc.
The Regent Mrs. Isabel M. W. Sears pre-
siding. There was a very good number of
members present and after miscellaneous
business had been attended to the name of
Mrs. Israel Evans of Lehi was proposed for
membership and she was admitted with a
hearty welcome as well as by unanimous
vote.


The paper of the day was given by Mrs.
Carol Y. Cannon, subject Mrs. John Jay.
The paper was exceptionally interesting,
well written and read in a clear musical
voice and delightfully gracious manner.
The secretary Mrs. Lucy W. Smith read
from a very old paper published soon after
the death ol General Washington, an ac-
count of the funeral exercises. Mrs. E. B.
Wells read a Poem in memory of General
Washington contained in the paper from
which the secretary read; the verses were
written by a young lady and may be
found on another page of this issue of the
paper.
	Mrs. Wells mentioned that the day pre-
vious had been Susan B. Anthony’s Birth-
day Anniversary and felt we should all
pay a tribute to her as she was most cer-
tainly a patriot loved her country and had
done much for the women of the United
States and the world at large.
	Many tender words of love and esteem
were expressed by the ladies and all unitedly
joined in congratulations to the honored
woman who has sacrificed so much and lab-
ored so faithfully for the enfranchisement
of the women of her country.
	Mrs. Beatie spoke of the visit of herself
and Mrs. Wells to the home of Miss Voss
in Indianapolis during their recent visit in
that city. Mrs. Beatie gave a graphic des-
cription of the elegant and luxurious resi-
dence of Miss Voss with whom the two
ladies dined and told of the many unique
hangings, statues, pictures and bric-a-brac
the lady had collected in her travels abroad.
	The meeting adjourned to meet on Wash-
ington’s birthday Feb. 22nd, at the EX-
PONENT OFFICE to commemorate the anni-
versary of the great hero, patriot, states-
man and President, Father of his Country.
Dainty refreshments were served by the
young ladies of the household and, with
each plate a toy hatchet marked Feb. 1904
	The afternoon was delightful in every
way, there was some choice music and
the usual pleasant conversation.


[Column 1]
EXECUTIVE SESSION N. C. W., INDIANAP-
			OLIS, INDIANA.

	The executive session of the National
Council of Women of the United States,
was held in Indianapolis, in February of
this year, commencing Monday, Eebruary
1, 1904.
	Mrs. Mary Wood Swift, of San Francisco,
the National President, presiding. Mrs.
Swift is very original; there is nothing for-
mal or stilted in her manner, she is easy
and gracious on the platform, and affable
and kind to all alike. The first afternoon,
as only a few delegates had yet arrived in
the city, was given over to a sort of “love
feast,” and almost every one present was
invited to speak and expressed an opinion
in regard to the Council, its present status,
and future possibilities, if the high ideals
of its founders were fostered and practically
developed.
	The four ladies from Utah gave free ex-
pression to the opinions they cherished on
the subject in question. The meeting was
held in the “Propylæum,” a handsome and
spacious building, erected and owned by
the club women of that city.
	In the evening a preliminary business
meeting was held in one of the elegant
parlors of the hotel Claypool, the headquar-
ters of the delegates in attendance at the

[Column 2]
Council. At this meeting propositions
were made and some plans formulated to
be submitted to the regular business sessions
during the week. One of the most inter-
esting things about the Council is the
women themselves who constitute the body
of the Council, its speakers and delegates.
To study these grand women who came to-
gether from many cities, and various or-
ganizations, representing a variety of lines
of work, and each with ideas of her own,
and plans to be submitted, furnishes charac-
ter-study of sufficient interest to make one
almost forget her own particular enterprise
in the endeavor to know human nature
better.
	Tuesday morning (closed session) the
work of the Executive began in earnest,
and continued during the week with
scarcely time to make one’s self comfortable
so strenuous was the effort to keep up with
the work in hand.
	The Young Woman’s Christian Associa-
tion very generously invited all the ladies
to lunch in their comfortable hall and every
day quite a number of ladies accepted their
hospitality. This was one of the many
polite courtesies extended to the Council
by the ladies of that hospitable city.
	At the close of the afternoon session
which was a continuance of Council bus-
iness, there was a “Tea” by the Fort-
nightly Club, given in the room across the
hall from the meeting, to which the ladies
repaired to meet and be introduced to these
earnest club women who had decorated
their room beautifully and prepared dainty
refreshments for the delectation of their
guests, the time was so limited that there
was very little opportunity for conversation,
and as it often happens the affair was
something of a disappointment, the meet-
ing having kept so late.
	In the evening; at 8 p. m., the meeting
was held in the large hall of the Propylæum,
a program prepared beforehand was given.
Addresses of welcome by Mrs. Kate Hunt-
ington Day, President of the local council
of Indianapolis, Hon. Winfried T. Durbin.
Governor of the State of Indiana, (who, by
the way, was not able to be present,) Hon.
John W. Holtzman, Mayor of the City of
Indianapolis, Judge Frank E. Gavin, Pres-
ident of the Commercial Club, of the City,
and Mrs May Wright Sewall, President
of the International Council of Women;
responses to addresses of welcome, Mrs.
Mary Wood Swift, President National
Council of Women, of the U. S.
	After the speeches a reception was given
by the Commercial Club and the local
Council, and dancing followed. The hall
was artistically decorated with flowers,
flags and banners, in profusion, presenting
a brilliant and gala appearance. The
music and dancing kept up till a, late hour,
though no one seemed weary either of
dancing or looking on.
	Wednesday morning, 9:30, session open
to all members of affiliated organizations
of the Local Council of the city. First came
minutes, then reports of committees and of
organizations affiliated with the Council.
	Mrs. Phebe Young Beatie, delegate gave
the report of the Relief Society published in
this issue of the paper. Mrs. Beatie made
an excellent impression upon the audience,
she spoke clearly and her enunciation was
perfect, the report was well received and
accorded considerable applause.
	The report of the Y. L N. M. I. A. came
next and was given without notes or paper
by Miss Agnes Campbell delegate for


[Column 1]
the Young Ladies’, she acquitted herself
creditably and we, Mrs Dougall and the
writer, were very proud of Utah delegates.
	The American Federation of nurses fol-
lowed. Miss Helena Bernard delegate. As
this is a subject of intense interest to us we
listened attentively as she gave an idea of
the work the nurses were doing as a feder-
ated body.
	Supreme Hive Ladies of the Maccabees
was given by Miss Bina M. West, Supreme
Record keeper, and when she gave the
financial condition of this far-reaching
society it made ones head swim, it included
so many thousands in treasury.
	The afternoon was given to reports first
being National Christian League for the
Promotion of Social Purity, President Mrs.
Elizabeth B. Grannis. National Association
of Colored Women Mrs. Davis delegate,
National Council of Jewish Women Mrs.
Hannah G. Solomon, this report was a
particularly fine one and in many respects
unequalled. Great Hive of the Ladies of
the Modern Maccabees, and several other
organizations were reported during the
afternoon but a number of the organiza-
tions were not representedat all.
	In the evening a reception was given by
Mrs. May Wright Sewall at her home, a
very brilliant affair, and very enjoyable;
many interesting people including a numb-
er of distinguished gentlemen doctors, law-
yers, professors etc.
	Possibly one of the Utah women might
give a good description of the elegant home,
old-fashioned and spacious, and of the bric-
a-brac ornaments paintings etc., but not the
writer, these things are not in her line. Day
followed day with vrork on committees and
in regular business sessions.
	Thursday afternoon was given up for a
trolley ride and to see the city, to be fol-
lowed by a “tea” to the National Executive
at the elegant home of Mrs John Candee
Dean, (a niece of Mrs. Sewall’s). Meeting
in the evening at the Propylaeum in the
large hall up-stairs, speakers, Mrs. Wyman
of the Relief Corps, G. A. R. Mrs. Krebs
of San Francisco, Grand Army of the Re-
public. Miss Louise Lee Hardin of the
Business Woman’s Journal and Mrs. C. C.
Bradford of Colorado, Miss. Ackerman on
the flag, Mrs. Sewall on Internationalism,
etc., until a very late hour.
	Friday morning 9:30 was to be given to
Local Councils Mrs. Kate Huntington Day
reported Local Council of Indianapolis, Mrs.
Maud H. Showers Local Council of Bloom-
ington and Mrs. Pauline Steinem Local
Council of Toledo, Ohio. Mrs. Grannis,
chairman of committee on Resolutions
brought in the report, the following were
adopted.
	That the Church universal be asked to
place women on an absolute equality with
men in the pew, the pulpit, in council and
in debate, and that the church elect from
one to one-half its officers and members of
committees from the women members.
	That excluding married women from
holding salaried positions in the public
schools is an adverse restriction and criticism
upon honorable wedlock, the rights and
privileges of wives and mothers and a cur-
tailing of the value of schools to the boy
and girl pupils and a wrong to the body po-
litic.
	That when a child is born out of wed-
lock, and the name ofi ts father is definitely
known by admission or proof, the child

[Column 2]
shall be entitled to the name of its father,
and shall share in his property the same
as children of legal parentage.
	That the National Council of Women
take such steps as are necessary to establish
juvenile courts in States where they do not
exist.
	That the members of the National Coun-
cil put themselves on record as advocating
servicable and hygienic dress, as opposed
to extremes of fashion or to forms of wear-
ing apparel which leave any doubt as to the
modesty of the wearer.
	The resolution which caused so much
discussion was as follows. (The writer so
far has not succeeded in finding the resolu-
tion as it was brought before the committee
of which she was a member also Mrs. Maria
Young Dougall but we give it here as it
was published after being modified).
	“Resolved, That any practice which un-
dermines the foundation of family life
should be strongly deprecated. and since
polygamous marriage is a terrible evil,
which threatens to destroy the home and
the state in portions of our country, the
people should petition and protest against
the seating in our national Congress any
man who may practice or subscribe to it.”
	Although the resolution was tabled the
women who favored it gave warning that
the subject would be voiced once more be-
fore the council made its final adjourn-
ment.
	Mrs. Wells and Mrs. Dongall strongly
protested against this resolution coming up
at all and urged that Congress had already
brought the matter before the Senate of the
United States and that the Senate would
and was sufficiently able to settle its own
affairs, but the advocates of the resolution
were persistent in bringing forward all the
stories current against Utah and its people
and declared the resolution was sent from
Salt Lake City by people who knew of the
continued practice of polygamy—and they
compared Senator Smoot’s case to that of
Breckenridge of Kentucky, etc. etc. It was
simply talking against the whirlwind so to
speak, but nevertheless we succeeded in
getting the Resolution tabled at last and
practically killed, although we had to listen
to the speeches of women who really knew
nothing of the question at issue, but were
fighting a phantom through misrepresenta-
tion of those who know not what they do.
	The newspapers say there were 6 against
tabling and 40 in favor of it, but there were
really only 5 against it just one eighth of
the assembly.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


[Column 1]
REPORT OF THE NATIONAL WOMAN’S
	RELIEF SOCIETY TO EXECUTIVE SESSION
	OF THE NATIONAL COUNCIL OF WOMEN
	IN INDIANAPOLIS, IND., FEBRUARY 3,
	1904.
	PRESENTED BY MRS. PHEBE YOUNG
				BEATIE
Madame President and Ladies of the Council:
	Since the report of the National Woman’s
Relief Society, at the Executive session of
the Council in New Orleans last March so
little time has elapsed that one has not
much to tell in the way of progress, yet in
the number of meetings and conferences
and in visits among the women of the Soci-
ety more has been done than during any
previous year.
	Our society has held over one hundred

[Column 2]
and fifty conferences; more than sixty of
these have been attended by the General
officers or members of the General Board
who travel in many instances long distances
and often by team over rough country
roads through mountain passes or canyons
to encourage, instruct and assist those who
are living far away from headquarters and
have fewer opportunities and advantages of
acquiring knowledge or understanding the
practical work of the Society along pro-
gressive lines.
	Our organization is charitable and phil-
anthropic and we reach out to the poor and 
needy and unfortunate, the sick and the
afflicted, yet we also try to teach the people 
how to live properly and how to avoid
much that tends toward dependence and
poverty by making better use of their time
and means.
	In the women’s meetings and conferences
we listen to reports of what has been done
in given localities and we thus cemprehend
better what the family life really is.
	When a local president reports her soci-
ety work in conference, she in a measure
represents herself and the people among
whom she dwells and with whom she labors
in the relief work.
	To hear these reports gives one a won-
derful knowledge of human nature, and
broadens one’s ideas of people and serves to
create an intense desire for better methods
of helpfulness; in fact the benefit is just
as great to the women who go out among the
people as it is to those who are visited.
	These visits to conferences during the
year have extended as far north as Canada,
and south in Mexico.
	Our last report stated that we had intro-
duced into our society in all its local
branches what we designate as mother’s
work. An outline of the subjects is pre-
pared by a competent committee and pre-
sented by women selected to lecture briefly
on the special subject; after the lecture
everyone present is at liberty to ask ques-
tions or discuss the subject and in this way
the lecture is explained and better under-
stood by all.
	This method has increased the member-
ship largely and brought more young
mothers into the society. The subjects
treated upon relate closely to marriage, its
puropse, etc., to prenatal influences, care of
the infant, the child, its rights and privil-
eges, obligation of parents, etc , home life,
necessity of obedience, industry, honesty
truthfulness, virtue, reverence for God and
sacred things, and all that will tend to make
life desirable and better in the home and
promote the welfare of the community.
	The education of trained nurses to care
for the sick who are needy and also as a
means of self-support to young women, in-
troduced as a department of Relief Society
work some years ago, has been most satis-
factory during the past year. Dr. Roberts,
who had been so successful heretofore in
this branch of relief work, has had a larger
number of students and from a wider area of
country than before. The class numbered
eighty-six, of this number seventy-five
graduated and received certificates from the
State Medical Board, as competent trained,
nurses, these young women were from
twenty-eight different counties in Utah and
some from adjacent states The course of
study includes physiology and hygiene also
domestic science as it relates to the sick
room. These nurses trained under the sup-
ervision of the Relief Society give a certain
specified amount of time among the sick


who are not able to pay for professional 
help. Mrs. Empey superintends the nurses 
work among the poor and has been very 
efficient in charge of this part of the depart-
ment and she also looks after the welfare
of the nurse herself when thus employed.
In this department of the society I have 
held the position of secretary and treasurer 
and have become greatly interested in the 
nurses and realize the immense benefit and 
blessing of such competent help among the 
poor and unfortunate and the spirit of true 
charity that is like the balm of Gilead to 
the soul of the distressed! Personally I en-
joy my labors in the Relief Society and re-
joice in the progress it is making; am proud 
that we are affiliated with this grand Conn-
cil of Women.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


FIRST WOMAN PHYSICIAN IN HOLLAND.
	On the 20th of March it will be twenty-
eight years since Dr. Aletta H. Jacobs, our
first lady doctor, took her degree. She
wss the first woman student at our univer-
ties, and had to go through all the un-
pleasant experiences which nowadays our
girls entering on a scientific course have no
occasion for. But she went on cheerfully,
and used her capacities in the service of
those who most heeded them. For some
years she gave gratis consultations for
women from the working classes in Am-
sterdam. Meanwhile she did not neglect
the women’s cause, and as she had made
the way for women into the medical pro-
fession, so she tried to do it into the larger
field of citizenship by demanding the bal-
lot, as well as her male colleagues. The
wise legislators of Holland then (1887) in-
serted the word “male” before voters in
the state constitution.
	Now, notwithstanding her busy life as a
practicing physician, Dr. Jacob is the pres-
dent of the Dutch Woman’s Suffrage As-
sociation, and one day may see her cause
triumph, when the proposed changes in the
constitution become law. She and her
husband, Mr. Gerritson, who as deputy and
alderman of Amsterdam, did much for
women’s employment, are true democrats,
working for peace and arbitration, and for
the uplifting of the degraded class and sex.
Dr. Jacobs now holds the office of vice-
president of the National Council of Women
of the Netherlands, and was recentiy elected
to be one of the delegates to represent her
national council at Berlin at the interna-
tional council meeting.
	The Dutch National Council of Women
will hold its annual meeting at the Hague
on April 5th. This year there will not be
reports from affiliated societies, for all the
three ensuing days will be taken up by the
congress on child protection, which is to
take place under the auspices of the Na-
tional Council. More than sixty associa-
tions who are interested in this work, and
consist of the most distinguished philan-
thropists of the country, have formed the
committee of arrangements for this con-
gress.
	On the first day (6th April), the care for
orphans and neglected children will be dis-
cussed by jurists and rescue workers; on
the second, the dangers for children at
school are to be considered by pedagogical
and medical authorities; and on the third,
the evil consequences of children leaving
school and being set to work too early are to
be pointed out by labor inspectors and
speakers from trades unions.
	As usual, the Dutch National Council of


Women extends a cordial invitation to
members of national councils in other coun-
tries, and would be glad to welcome those
who understand the language in the Hague
on the 5th of April.
	I should be glad if they could bring con-
gratulations to my friend on March 10th,
and visitors to our council on April 5th.
		Yours sincerely,
			MARTINA G. KRAMERS.
Rotterdam, February 10, 1904.


RELIEF SOCIETY NURSE CLASS.
	Will commence in September 1904. Four
lessons a week, continuing until 1905, In-
structor, Dr. Margaret C. Roberts. There
will also be a course in cooking and lectures
on important subjects by ladies chosen from
or by the General Board of the Relief Soci-
ety. Terms for tuition, including hall and
janitor expenses, ten dollars per student.
	Students will be obliged to furnish their
own note books, text books and pencils.
	The students shall each sign a contract
to nurse for charity for seventy-five days
or whatever pay shall be designated by the
superintendent for two years from the date
of graduation.
	Any one desiring to take the nurse
course and not paid by the Relief Society of
her ward may do so by paying the ten dol-
lar fee herself and signing the contract. All
correspondence on this subject should be
addressed to Mrs. Phebe Y. Beatie, 55 N.
West Temple Street.
				EMMA A. EMPEY,,
Superintendent Relief Society Nurse Class.


[Column 1]
		THE SUEZ CANAL.
Editor Exponent:
	You ask me to give you a few particulars
relative to my journey through the Suez
canal in 1874.
	We started from Bombay in one of the
Peninsula & Oriental Company’s Steamers
and arrived at Suez; which is the port of
Egypt on the Red sea and southern term-
inus of the Suez canal.
	The site is naturally an absolute desert
and until the water of the Nile was intro-
duced by a fresh water canal in 1863 the
water supply of Suez was brought across
the head of the gulf from the wells of
Moses on the Arabian coast or else carried
on camels, an hour’s journey from the
brackish wells of “Bic Survees.”
Suez itself is a most uninteresting place
with its mean Kozace or rows of native
shops and Mosques and its Mongrel popu-
lation. The only thing of interest to
travelers is the variety of dirty costumes
and the splendid view of the Sinai mount-
ains on its eastern, and Mount Alako on
its western shore.
	It was indeed a great achievement to re-
duce the distance between Western Europe
and India from 11,378 miles to 7,628
miles, equal to saving 36 days voyage (es-
pecially to sufferers by sea sickness), and
this was the result effected by cutting the
Suez canal between the Red sea and the
Mediterranean. Great credit and an ever-
lasting name is due Monsieur Ferdinand de
Lesseps, who was the great engineer who
brought the work to a successful issue.
From 1849 to 1854 he was occupied in ma-
turing his plans.
	He constituted a company called the Uni-
versal Suez Canal Company. It was not

[Column 2]
until the year 1860 that the work was act-
ually commenced. The canal extends from
Suez on the Red Sea to Port Said on the
Mediterranean. In going through the
Suez canal we pass through three lakes
(“Truly in the desert a fountain is spring-
ing, in the wild waste there still is a tree.”)
	Lake Menzaleh is nearest to the Mediter-
ranean. Lake Timsah is about one-half
way across the isthmus, fresh water, and
about five miles long, and. the Bitter Lake
(called so from the bitter taste of the water)
about twenty-three miles long. There are
buoys placed for the ships to pass between
(as they are very wide in some places—ten
miles and even more) so that it is necessary
to have some means to direct the course of
the ships.
	The whole length of the canal after pass-
ing through the locks, etc., at the entrance
and exit, is sixty-six miles. At every five
or six miles there is a passing place to en-
able large vessels to moor during the night,
and to bring up for other vessels to pass.
	All vessels not mails have to draw into
these passing places to allow the mails to
pass through without any delay. This
courtesy is accorded to mails of every
nation.
	Dredging machines are constantly at
work, as the shifting sands would other-
wise make the canal unnavigable.
	During the cutting of the canal fresh
water was conveyed from the Nile at Cairo
and distributed along the whole length of
the canal, a work which of itself was of no
small magnitude. The cost of the whole
works is stated to be about £20,000,000.
	The canal is a highway for steamers of
400 feet in length and 50 feet wide. It takes
three days to pass from Suez to Fort Said.
	The following table will give you some
idea of the use of the canal from 1870 to
1874, and of course the use and reveuue
arising from it now must be much greater:
Year		No. of Ships	Tonnage		Receipts
1870		486		654,915		206,373

	The tonnage was thus quadrupled in five
years.
	As far as the eye can see there is nothing
but sand and high hillocks of it in some
places like little mountains, but since the
canal has been cut there is vegetation of
mild growth, but it gave me the impression
even then that with fresh water the desert
might yet fulfill the words of scripture and
blossom like the rose.
	I knew nothing in those days of this won-
derful western desert that has literally ful-
filled the promise. I had the pleasure of
seeing for the first time a mirage. I said
to the Captain surely that is a town and
fields we see, and he allowed me to use his
powerful glass, but it had the same indis-
tinct appearance, and he told me it was a
mirage and that sometimes they were much
plainer than this one.
	Port Said is one of the dirtiest places on
earth. When I tell you all the water is
brought from the Sweetwater canal at
Ismailia by a conduit and is barely sufficient
for the wants of the town, and to add to its
dirt and misery it is a coaling station for the
P. & O. Steamers, you can better imagine
than I can describe the dirt and filth of the
place.
	A party of us went to look around the
town and paid a visit to one of their Casino


Smoking Concerts. We had no idea what 
such a place would be like and as we all
had gentlemen with ns we were safe. Arab
and Italian women played the most aston-
ishing instruments, and altogether the 
whole affair was so disgusting we did not 
stay many minutes, but strangers like to 
see all they can in a foreign place. There 
had been no time for our escorts to investi-
gate before entering as we had only a short 
time to remain on shore while the steamer 
was coaling, and they will not wait a second 
when they are ready.
	Passing through the canal is a break to 
the monotony of the voyage and makes the
passengers very sociable. Although a jour-
ney from England to India is very enjoy-
able, and I should not mind making the 
voyage again to re-visit my beloved India.
With kindest regards.
			Yours Very Sincerely,
					MARIA FRANCIS.

[Column 1]
NOTES ABOUT WOMEN.
	Miss Florence Hayward has been select-
ed by the St. Louis World’s Fair to col-
lect the material for the exhibit in the his-
torical section of the department of anthro-
pology.
	Mrs. Lucretia Willard Treat, widely
known as a kindergarten lecturer, writer
and organizer, is dead. She was principal
of the Training School for Kindergarten
Teachers, Grand Rapids, Mich.
	Mrs. Isabella Beecher Hooker of Hartford,
Conn., was eighty-two years old on Feb.
22. “I am full of life and feel as though I
should live to be a hundred,” she say, in
response to congratulations. The birthday
was observed by a family gathering in the
evening.
	Queen Wilhelmina inaugurated the fest-
vities attending the queen mother’s jub-
lee with a gala dinner at The Hague rec-
ently. Speaking for herself and for the
Prince Consort, she proposed the health of
the queen mother, whose life, she said, had
been prolonged, “not for herself alone,
but, by the grace of God, for her people,
who adored her.”
	The Empress of Germany, the Princess
Henry, and others of the royal household
have promised to attend the Quinquennial
of the International Council of Women in
Berlin next summer. The Berlin Central
Committee has for its active chairman Mrs.
Hedwig Heyl, wife of the Imperial Coun-
cillor of Commerce.
	Princess Wiszniewski, who lately died
in Paris, founded in 1896 the Universal
Alliance of Women for Peace, and was its
president. Many American women are en-
rolled among its nearly 6,000,000 members.
	The maiden name of the princess was
Hugot, and she was a native of Bergundy.
	For more than thirty years she has dev-
oted herself to the cause of peace, ably as-
sisted by her husband, Prince Wiszniewski.
	Miss Agnes Mahony, a missionary who
is to return to West Africa next month,
will take with her a complete house, crated in
sections, and ready to be set up in the wilds
as a home for herself, a hospital for the
sick and a chapel for her converts. The
house is of Yellow pine, with a roof of
corrugated iron. It is forty feet long,

[Column 2]
twenty feet wide, and eight feet to the
eaves. It is to be set up near Cape Mount,
Sierra Leone.
		Ex. Boston Woman’s Journal.
			IN MEMORIAM.
		DEATH OF A NOBLE WOMAN.
	A bright spirit passed to the “other side” when
Sister Elizabeth M. Hart departed from mortality
on Friday January 15, 1904, death came without
warning and all were unprepared for the sad
event.
	Sister Hart has been a member of the Relief
Society Board of the Bear Lake Stake for nearly
twenty years, having been chosen 2nd Counselor
to President Julia P. Lindsay, July 13, 1884, and
1st Counselor, June 11, 1899, which position she
held at the time of her death. She was also Stake
Secretary of the Y. L. M. I. A. for many years.
	Our dear Sister has left a host of sorrowing
friends, and in the hearts of her co-laborers there
are thoughts which lie too deep for tears or words.
	Through her refined nature she could lead others
to higher tastes and nobler conduct. She carried
with her a sweet spirit of peace, and possessed the
power of winning hearts by her genial manner.
Her great desire was to remain true to the Gospel
of Christ; this she has done, and has labored
devotedly for the advancement of the work of
God, her example shines for emulation by the
daughters of Zion.
	It is a great thing to be ready at the Master’s
“call” with the work allotted us “well done.”
	The funeral services were held in the Paris
Tabernacle on Monday the 18th.
	The obsequies were in every way appropriate.
	Among the speakers was our beloved and aged
President Julia P. Lindsay who was grief stricken
at the thought of parting with one with whom she
had labored harmoniously for years past.
	The music was heavenly and inspiring. A
beautiful selection was rendered by the choir en-
titled “Thy Will Be Done,” and a Solo by Elder
Jos. R. Shepherd, “He Giveth His Beloved
Sleep.”
	The floral decorations were profuse and beauti-
ful.

		Gone! sweet spirit to our Father,
		Gone to join with saints above.
		She has nobly filled her mission
		She has done a work of love.

		She has left us for a season
		And earth’s temple laid to rest,
		But the soul, Ah! its immortal
		And is mingling with the blest.

		We have lost a faithful sister
		She has reached the radiant shore
		We must labor on without her
		’Til earth’s pilgrimage is o’er.

		Then shall we all meet in heaven
		In that home we loved of yore,
		There unite with severed spirits
		With dear friends to part no more.
						NANCY H. ALLRED.

VALENTINE’S DAY.
	According to some ecclesiastical writers,
St. Valentine was a bishop, according to
others a presbyter. He was beheaded at
Rome in the reign of the Emperor Claud-
ius, 270 A. D., and was early canonized.
Wheatley says: “He was a man of most
admirable parts, and so famous for his love
and charity that the custom of choosing
valentines upon his festival took its rise
from thence.” Others derive the custom
from birds being supposed to select their
mates on this day; others from a custom
practised in ancient Rome at the festival of
the Supercalio, during the month of Feb-
ruary, when among other ceremonies, the
names of young women were placed in a
box, from which they were drawn by men
as chance directed, with the same merri-
ment that valentines are exchanged to-
day.
	The pastors of the early church, finding
it impossible to extirpate this heathen cere-
mon changed the form. [---] he cus-
tom on the eve of St. Valentine to have
the names of a select number of one sex
put into some vessel by an equal number
of the other, and thereupon everyone drew
a name which for the time being was called
his or her valentine.
	The custom of choosing valentines exist-
ed very early. Presents of gloves, garters
and jewelry were common as valentines.
						C. J. T.
[Written for Reaper’s Club.]

POETRY—When the Lilac Comes...................	73
	My Sister Spirit.........................................	75


WHEN THE LILAC COMES.
When the lilac comes and the young birds sing,
And the violet shows her purple wing,
And the primrose coppice is fair to see,
Like a yellow cloud in an em’rald sea—
Oh, my heart leaps up like a wild bird free—
	When the lilac comes!
When the lilac comes, with its wealth of white,
And helio'rope radiance all alight.
With a breath as sweet as the new-mown hay
That rests in twilight at hush of day,
And the acacia uncurls outside my room
With a promise of future life and bloom—
Then my heart like a bird, begins to sing—
	When the lilac comes!
When the lilac comes and the sun looks down
On laburnum chains and the snowdrop’s gown,
And the pink-eyed daisies look up to the skies.
And there’s laughter of winds in place of sighs,
And the dimpled brooklet that sings as it flows
’Neath the bramble bush and the budding rose—
Than my spirit laughs like a child at play—
	When the lilac comes!
When the lilac comes—in the garden wide
There’s many a blossom the wall besi
That show a glimmer of pink-streaked white
That peeps from its cover to find the light;
And a butterfly bright is sometimes seen.
Like a golden star, near the ivy green;
And from the root of the old oak tree
Tha daffodils golden smile up at me—
My heart is as glad as a heart can be—
	When the lilac comes!
When the lilac comes, our hearts are athrill
With the voice of the cuckoo adown the hill;
And, lo, it is spring in t e gleaming air,
And, lo, it is spring in our spirits fair
And my heart o’erflows as the brook o’erflows
When it hurries away to find the rose;
And my heart o’erflows as a golden sea
Of sunshine over a cowslip lea.
Oh! the world is as fair as a world can be—
	When the lilac comes!
					M. A. Beckingham.

SALT LAKE STAKE CONFERENCE.
	The Relief Society of Salt Lake Stake
was held in the Assembly Hall, Saturday,
March 5, Counselor Sarah Jenne Cannon
presiding. Singing, ‘‘How firm a founda-
tion,” etc. Prayer by Sister Elizabath
Paul. Singing, solo, ‘‘Only Remembered
for What We have Done,” etc. Roll call,
minutes of previous conference read and
accepted. Counselor S. J. Cannon made an
opening address, exhorted the sisters to
faithfulness and referred to the noble ex-
ample and heroism of President Horne,
paid a loving tribute to her worth and re-
gretted she could not be present with the

[Column 2]
sisters with whom she had labored so long.
Reports were given from 1st, 2nd, 3rd,
4th, 5th, 6th, 7th, 8th, 9th, 10th, 11th,
13th, 14th and 15th wards. Solo, ‘‘Some
Day,” by Sister Maggie C. Hull.
	Counselor Annie Taylor Hyde of the
General Presidency, addressed the sisters;
referred to the good condition financially,
of the Society in the stake as manifest by
the reports given, felt the change in the
Stake would be a good thing, strengthen-
ing the Church, giving the young a chance
to come forward and take part, each one
developing himself or herself, as well as
helping one another; referred to the faith-
fulness of President Horne, how the Lord
had blessed her always in giving wise
counsel; ‘:we should learn to control our-
selves, we should set good examples; this
life is a proving place for us all; be careful
of our children in all things; be careful how
we judge one another, but be charitable to
each other; we should pray for our presi-
dent.”
	Reports, 16th,17th, 18th, 19th, 20th
wards. Meeting adjourned by singing
‘‘We thank Thee, O God,” etc. Prayer
by Sister Julia C Howe.
	Afternoon session, Counselor S. J Can-
non presiding. Singing, ‘‘Now let us re-
joice.” etc. Prayer, Sister Mary A. C.
Lambert. Singing, quartette; minutes
read and accepted. — Address from Presi-
dent M Isabella Horne to the sisters, read
by Sister Annie Wells Cannon,
PRESIDENT M. ISABELLA HORNE’S
			ADDRESS.
My Beloved Sisters:
	This conference is, as you all know, the
last conference of the Relief Society of what
is now the Salt Lake Stake of Zion, and
the time seems to me, most opportune to
send you my heartfelt greeting, and to
express to you some of my feelings in the
form of an address, reminiscent of our past,
relating to our present and concerning our
future as Relief Society workers
	In some respects this is a sad occasion,
for it means a parting of our ways: Here-
after while still engaged, I hope, in the
same line of work, it will be under differ-
ent  fficers and different names, and we
will meet no more in the same capacity but
hope to meet with you all agaiu in what
ever stakes you may be. I feel attached to
you, every one, with the most tender feel
ings, and shall always look upon your work
with great interest, feeling towards the new
stakes like a mother to her children as they
leave the home and begin making homes
for themselves. There is mingled in my
heart, with the sadness of this occasion,
great joy and satisfaction when I recall the
wonderful work that has been done and the
great amount of good accomplished by you,
my sisters, in thi[-] organization.
	Too much praise cannot be given you for
your untiring labors. Out of nothing but
your own energy and hard work you have
builded granaries, storehouses and meeting-
houses. By your gentle miuisterings to
the sick and afflicted, the poor and the

[Column 3]
needy your great helpfulness to the bishops
in building up their wards, mingled with
your wonderful testimonies of the faith,
you are indeed rich, and great will be your
reward and many the blessings that will
come to you for your labors in these re-
spects.
	Relief Society work has been a part of my
life for the past sixty years or more, for I
attended the meetings of the first Relief
Society organized by the Prophet Joseph
Smith in Nauvoo and I wish to testify to
you, my sisters, that Joseph Smith was in-
spiried of God when he effected the organ-
zation of the Relief Society, and it is one
of the most important parts of the Church.
It is so recorded in the book of ‘‘The law
of the Lord.”
	At the meeting in the Tabernacle when
the general organization was made and Sis-
ter Eliza R. Snow was made president,
Brother John Taylor, then the presiding
officer of the Church, had Brother L. John
Nuttall bring to the meeting this precious
book and read from its pages to those pres-
ent, thus showing that this organization of
the sisters was of great importance, for it
was ordained by the Lord; and it is not
sufficient, my sisters, that your names are
on the books as members only, and that
occasionally you respond when called upon
with some slight donation; for we will be 
judged from the books. Zion must work
most diligently, both spiritually and prac-
tically. It is the divine purpose of this or-
ganization that we may scatter the fruits of
our labors where they are needed.
	Twenty-six years ago last December,
while spending a few days at the home of
Sister Elizabeth Howard in Big Cotton-
wood, in company with Sister Eliza R.
Snow, Emmeline B. Wells and others, in a
preliminary way the organization of this
stake was talked over. I recall this instance
now, because Big Cottonwood, as well as
all the wards of the new stakes south of
the city, now Granite and Jordan were a
part of this stake, and there was then some-
years later in Salt Lake Stake more than
fifty branches.
	It was on the 22nd of December, 1877,
that a special meeting was called to organ-
ize the Salt Lake Stake Relief Society.
Bishop Edward Hunter was present with
others of the breth en. Sister Eliza R.
Snow presided. It was then I was called
to be your president and from that day to
the present it has been my most earnest de-
sire to encourage the sisters, both at our
conferences, which we have always held
every three mouths, and during my visits
to the different wards, to continue in th s
great, benevol nt work with all the energy
they could command
	In looking over our present condition we
have much indeed to be thankful for. Our
reports show us to be in a very prosperous
condition. Eight of our wards own Relief
Society halls, and eight own granaries. We
have considerable means on hand, both
cash and merchandise and I am pleased to
state are all th[-] time disbursing m ans to
help others. Through these organizations
we have emigrated many people to Zion,


[Column 1]
found homes for the orphan and the friend-
less and have contributed liberally towards
the building and maintenance of the Tem-
ple and houses of worship, and have, I be-
lieve, endeavored to promote all the inter-
ests of Zion. Towards the Woman’s Build-
ing the Salt Lake Stake was assessed two
thousand dollars, and this we have more
than paid; the general treasurer states we
have given two thousand, one hundred and
fifty-six dollars and sixteen cents. Still
some of the wards are delinquent. The
amount was proportioned among the differ-
ent wards and some have paid in more than
required while others are behind. The
stake record, therefore, may look all right,
more than satisfying, yet, my sisters, do
not allow your wards to go out of the stake
in debt. See to it, each one of you, that
the required sum is made up before you be-
come a part of another stake.
	While our officers’ meetings have been
very, well attended, and great interest has
been shown, our conferences have been
rather discouraging in this respect. The
members do not seem to feel the necessity
of attending and think the society will be
all right if the president or some officer goes
so as to get instruction. Now, my sisters,
this is not right. In order to keep in
touch with the work of this organization,
and receive the benefits thereof, you must
attend your meetings and I hope the aver-
age attendance in your soeiety will here-
after improve. As it is our record shows
only, out of an enrollment of about eighteen
hundred an attendance of six hundred. I
do not wish to find fault on this occasion,
and only mention these things in a kindly
way to urge upon you greater diligence in
your future work. Through revelation
from God the Prophet Joseph Smith organ-
ized the fisrt society of sisters in this last
dispensation. He gave the sisters much
valuable instruction and predicted a great
future for the Relief Society. As soon as
possible after the location of the Saints in
these valleys, President Brigham Young
counseled tho organizing of these societies
in all the stakes of Zion and also predicted
for the sisters a great future. Today we
see these predictions fulfilled, for the Relief
Society is one of the largest and grandest
organizations of women in the world and
by the united efforts of the sisters the good
accomplished cannot be estimated. Con-
tinue, my dear sisters, in your good works
help each other with kind, friendly feelings,
with faith and prayer. Try to promote all
the interests of Zion, by sustaining home
industries and home literature. Remem-
ber the EXPONENT is the organ of our or-
ganization and enlightens you in regard to
Relief Society work. Read the Church
works and carry their teachings in your
own lives and instil them into the lives of
your children. Do not be indifferent about
storing and caring for the grain. Remem-
ber we were commanded by our beloved
leader, Brigham Young, to make this a
part of our work, and I feel to urge upon
you the necessity of attending to this matter
for I judge by the signs of the times, that
before long we will be called upon to open
our granaries, and great will be our shame
if they are empty, or if we have neglected
this duty. We were told through revelation
to save the grain against a day of famine.
Other prophecies are being fulfilled, why
should this one not? Let us be prepared,
sisters for this day, and let the women of
Zion be prepared for all emergencies and
not be like the foolish virgins with their

[Column 2]
empty lamps, but be ready and waiting in
full faith for the coming of the bridegroom.
	Now, as we are about to separate in our
work in this stake, take with you, my dear
sisters, my heartfelt blessing. May the
peace and the blessing of God be in the
hearts and habitations of all who are inter-
ested in the work of the Lord.
	Your sister and colaborer in ihe Gospel,
			M. ISABELLA HORNE.
	General President Bathsheba W. Smith
gave some instructions and expressed her
feelings upon Relief Society work; exhorted
the sisters to live up to the principles of
the Church and felt that good was being
accomplished all over the world through
this grand society, spoke tenderly of Sister
Horne and their long acquaintance and as-
sociation together and felt she had been a
wise woman.
	Counselor Clara C. Cannon who had
been long in the stake with Sister Horne,
said she had enjoyed being with the sisters,
felt that the change in the stake would be
a great benefit to the young in giving them
something to do in the Relief Society work;
she had been so many years associated in
the work that she felt sad in having the
change take place; urged all to be honest
and diligent in doing all they could to roll
on the work.
	Sister E. B. Wells paid a glowing tribute
to President Horne, referred to her mani-
fold labors for half a century and more,
she having been a member of the Society in
Nauvoo; spoke of her great activity in
many ways and many places, her travels at
home and abroad, and gave a sort of his-
tory of the Salt Lake Stake organization,
its officers a[-]d what had been accomplished
in many directions, home industries, etc.
	Solo, Sister M. C. Hull. Sister Clarissa S.
Williams said our interests were still the
same to our fellow laborers, expressed her
love for Sister Horne and many sisters she
had become acquainted with, thought there
was a great work for us to do; responsibility
is a great benefit, it causes us to make ex-
tra efforts when we otherwise would not,
thought the society would increase more
rapidly in the future, double what it had
done in the past fifty years.
	Sister Priscilla P. Jennings said she
missed Sister Horne, felt to rejoice in the
Gospel, referred to President Smith being
absent from us; she believed all would be
well, the change in the stake would be a
benefit to all.
	Sister Lydia D. Alder: “We have love
for one another, referred to the ordeal of
President Smith, felt she knew the influ-
ence of such conditions for she had wit-
nessed such trials herself she realized the
importance of Relief Society work.
	Sister Emily S. Richards said she remem-
bered Sister Horne many years gone by,
she had tried to take part in the society in
her young life, thought we should pray for
Sister Horne.
	Sister A. W. Cannon read a notice of a
Nurse Class to commence in September,
Dr. Margaret C. Roberts, Instructor.
	Sister E. B. Wells made some explana-
tions in regard to the National and Inter-
national Councils and moved that the as-
sembly send love and greeting to President
Horne, and by a rising vote express thanks
to those who had assisted in helping to
make the conference a success. Mentioned
Prof. C. J. Thomas and others who had
been helpful, Sister Horne’s counselors,
aids, secretaries and treasurers, and all who

[Column 3]
had labored so efficiently during the admin-
istration of President Horne. The motion
was seconded by Sister Mary Alice Lam-
bert and all on the stand and carried unan-
imously by a rising vote. Singing by a
quartette. Counselor Sarah J. Cannon an-
nounced the conference would be adjourned
forever, so far as she knew.
	Brief remarks in loving words for Presi-
dent Horne by Sister Elizabeth J. Steven-
son, who also pronounced the benediction.
		JULIA S. WOOLLEY, Sec.

WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


MY SISTER SPIRIT.
TO EMMELINE B. WELLS.

Oh methinks in worlds afar, sweet sister spirit
	mine—
We knew and loved each other with tenderness
	divine;
That hand in hand together we roamed Elysian
	grove.
Heart beating close to heart with pure supernal
	love.

As children of one Father, our great eternal One!
We waited there His pleasure, we lingered near
	His throne;
Until at length He called us, angel spirits to this
	earth.
To obtain our mortal bodies, thus to gain im-
	mortal birth.

How infinite His wisdom, how great His good-
	ness too.
That still we can together His wondrous works 
	pursue.
That we congenial spirits may labor in His
	cause,
Thus gain eternal blessings by obeying sacred
	laws.

When life’s mission we have finished—our weak-
	nesses o’ercome.
With affection undiminished, may we meet again
	at Home.
May our crowns of endless glory with unceasing
	lustre shine
As reward for this probation, O, sweet sister spirit
	mine.
						ELLIS R. SHIPP.
February 29, 1904.


WOMEN PHYSICIANS.
	At a recent meeting of the Historical
Society of Lynn, Mass., Mrs. May L. Shel-
don read a paper on “The Doctors of
the Old Town.” She said that the first
doctor recorded in the history of Lynn was
Deborah Armitage, who in early colonial
times trudged from Salem through Lynn
to Malden with her bag of drugs, herbs
and lotions, ministering to the sick of all
classes. For over thirty years she gath-
ered herbs from the pastures, roadside
and swamp, made her salves and lotions,
and put her healing powers to use. She
was devoted to duty, and yielded her life
to its demands. In January, 1860, there
came a heavy snowfall which lasted for sev-
eral days and covered her humble cottage.
When the neighbors came to dig away the
snow, they found her absent. A search
revealed her stiffened body in the snow
near Saugus, where she had been on her
way to minister to a patient.
	Madame Maria Martin in Le Journal des
Femmes for February, has a brilliant arti-
cle on “The Education of the Father.”
She points out that the world has had
abundant preaching on the duties of moth-
ers and it is now time that the attention
of fathers should be called to their duties
to their children, especially in the way of
setting them a good example.


LADY WARWICK AND DAUGHTER.
	Rarely would a wedding in “high life”
abroad concern the readers of this paper,
but an exception would certainly be made
in the recent marriage of Lady Marjorie to
Viscount Helmsley. Marjorie Gervile, as
her mother wished her to be known during
her school days, is the daughter of the Coun-
tess of Warwick, long regarded as the
most beautiful woman in England, and
known the world over for her participa-
tion in industrial and social reforms. As
owner and editor of an agricultural paper,
speaker at socialist congresses, founder of


trades, schools and shops for young women
and in many other ways, she has identified
herself with the interests of the workers.
The latest of the philanthropic schemes is
the purchase of the “Saracen’s Head,” an
inn near her country place in Essex, which
she has now converted into a temperance
tavern, it is said greatly to the benefit of
the surrounding country as it was the only
place in the country where liquor could be
procured.
In the education of her daughter Lady
Warwick has proceeded according to
the same democratic ideals that she advo-
cates for others. Marjorie was sent to the
public schools in childhood and allowed to
play with village children, while at her
home she had a garden and poultry to
care for, a Jersey cow to milk, and was
taught practical dairying and agriculture
and all outdoor sports. Lady Marjorie was
presented at court last year and was at
once regarded as the prettiest peeress in
the kingdom and now she is married to the
heir of one of the historic earldoms of Eng-
land with all the pomp and circumstance
befitting her ancestral home, grand old
Warwick Castle, which no person visits
England without seeing if possible. King
Edward sent the bride a valuable present
with an autograph letter with best wishes
for her happiness, and in this we all may
join, and those who remember Lady War-
wick’s participation in the reception given
by her sister, the Duchess of Sutherland,
to the International Council of Women will
give the heartiest amen.	Ex.


D.R. MEETINGS.
WASHINGTON’S BITHDAY.	
	The Daughters of the Revolution met
Wednesday, February 22, in the EXPONENT
office, at 11 a.m., to commemorate the an-
niversary of General George Washington's
birthday. The Regent, Mrs. Isabel M. W.
Sears, presided. Each one present brought
some sentiment, original or selected, or
spoke a few words as a tribute to the mem-
ory of one whom all true Americans delight
to honor; the stars and stripes were dis-
played conspicuously and other decorations
adorned the walls of the room. Mrs. Bath-
sheba W. Smith, herself an enthusiastic
Virginian, felt proud of her state as the
home of this great and good man, and eulo-
gized his life and labors; at the close of
the reading of sentiments and remarks by
others, including some visitors, Mrs. Mary
McNaughtan and Mrs. Mary Smith Wim-
mer, also Mrs. C. Willis Schow, Mrs. C.
H. Y. Spencer read a poem on General
Washington, written by Miss Josephine
Spencer, which added greatly to the spirit
of the occasion. The meeting opened and
closed with prayer.

	On Wednesday, March 17, the ladies of
the D. R. held their regular monthly meet-
ing, at 3 p.m., in Room 509 Templeton
Building. The Regent in the chair. After
the opening exercises and reading of min-
utes, considerable business was transacted,
and the program of the day carried out.
Mrs. John Henry Smith gave a very enter-
taining and valuable paper on Alexander
Hamilton, which was greatly appreciated,
and she was followed by Mrs. Lucy W.
Smith on Aaron Burr; this paper brought
out many striking attributes of Aaron
Burr’s character hitherto unknown to the


ladies of the Society. Mrs. John D. Spen-
cer read a letter from Mr. Burr’s daughter
to her father, showing her devotion to and
admiration of this man of destiny, reveal-
ing the strong affection between father and
child. Mrs. Spencer also read some ex-
tracts from Blennerhassett, showing phases
of the man’s character entirely different to
the general opinion. The discussion of the
papers was quite edifying. Adjourned un-
til April 16, to convene at Mrs. T. G. Web-
ber’s, 131 Second Sireet. Prayer by the
chaplain.

president of the N. C. W. of the U. S.,
will be pleased to see the following notice
of a five o'clock Tea given by her recently
in the historic city of Rome, where she is
spending the winter, her daughter, Miss
Jayta, is with her there. Miss Humph-
reys is the granddaughter of the famous
late Dr. Humphreys who visited Salt Lake
in the spring of 1898 and wrote a book en-
titled "A Flight in Spring." His little
granddaughter was with him on that jour-
ney.

Among all the great people who have
been born in February, Mrs. Isabella
Beecher Hooker is one of the most notable.
On Monday, Feb. 22, she attained the age
of eighty-two and the fact was duly regarded
by the citizens of Hartford, where she has
lived for fifty-four years. Mrs. Hooker is
the last of the distinguished sons and
daughters of Rev. Lyman Beecher, D. D.,
and all of those who gathered lovingly
around Mrs. Hooker at the family banquet
were of the younger generations, children
and grandchildren, nephews and nieces.
Mrs. Hooker is still in active suffrage work
and says she feels as if she should live to 
be a hundred years old. 			Ex.
