tions of the Relief Society in Salt Lake Co.
Liberty, Pioneer, Salt Lake and Ensign, as
yet no Conferences of these Relief Society
organizations have been held, but will be
probably in the near future.

beth J. Stevenson have been appointed by
the General President to attend Cache Stake
Relief Society Conference at Logan, April
30, and Hyrum Stake Conference May 2,
1904.

lief Society Conference of the Blackfoot
Stake of Zion was held in Shelley, Idaho,
the newly elected President Ann H. Walk-
er presiding. Sisters Emily S. Richards
and Priscilla Smith representatives from the
General Board attended the Conference and
gave instructions.
	By invitation of the President of Relief
Society Morgan Stake, Sisters Mary Alice
C. Lambert and Lydia D. Alder attended
the Relief Society Conference of that Stake
of Zion, on Sunday, April 17. Sister Eliza-
beth J. Stevenson attended the Relief So-
ciety Conference of Davis Stake held at
Centerville, April 28, by invitation.
	We have on hand minutes of many Re-
lief Society Conferences and Seventeenth of
March celebrations, we are glad the sisters
send them in and such condensed minutes
as we can find space for will be used, but
the material is at hand to help make history,
and therefore it is not sent in vain. The
Outlines of Mother’s Work have taken
much of the space in our little paper and
we trust our sisters understand and appre-
ciate this fact as it has been with a view of
helping the branches in their respective lo-
calities.


BRIEF HISTORY OF THE STAKE BOARD
	PRIMARY ASSOCIATION OF THE SALT
	LAKE STAKE.

	After the first organization of the Primary
Associations by Sister Eliza R. Snow, the 
associations increased so rapidly throughout
the Salt Lake Stake of Zion that it was de-
cided to organize in a Stake capacity.
	The first stake organization was effected
on June 19, 1880, Ellen C. Clawson being
chosen as president, with Camilla C. Cobb
as first and Annie Davis (now Watson) as
second counselors, and Vilate Young as
secretary. Shortly after Lydia A. Wells
was appointed second counselor, Annie
Davis having resigned
	The sisters held these positions until the 
death of Sister Clawson which occurred in
1896, she having held the office of president
for seventeen years, during which time a


[Column 1]
number of changes had been made in the
secretaryship, Vilate Young was followed
by Caroline C. Raleigh, as secretary, after
which Bessie Dean was appointed. Ida
Whipple followed Sister Dean and held the
position until 1896, when she offered her
resignation. During the seventeen years
of the existence of this organization great
progress had been made in the advancement,
of Primary work in the Salt Lake Stake
	On September 12, 1896, a reorganization
of the Stake Board was effected. Camilla
C. Cobb having been a very efficient work-
er was selected as president, and she chose
for her counselors Lydia A Wells and
Mary L. Morris, with Ella W. Hyde as
secretary. Catherine Wells as treasurer,
Julia C. Howe and Ida Whipple Taylor as
aids.
	As the associations multiplied and the
Primary work increased, President Cobb
found it necessary to call more aids to her
assistance, and on June 5, 1897 she ap-
pointed Jane S. Hill and Lauretta Corn-
wall as aids to visit the country wards.
	On May 7, 1898, she also appointed as
aids to the Stake Board, Jessie P. Jones,
Fanny Wool[-]ey, Mary A. Tudenham and
Eliza Bergen, and soon after this Millie P.
Bassett and Clara Beebe were added to the
list of aids. As the work advanced, more
aids were required and on May 4, 1900.
Betsy Howarth and Josephine Woolley
were appointed.
	On August 4, 1900, the organist, Sister
Laura Cushing, having resigned, Lizzie
Thomas was appointed stake organist. On
March 2, 1901, Mary L. Morris offered her
resignation as second counselor in the Stake
Board on account of ill health, and Fanny
Woolley was appointed in her place, and in
September of the same year, Sister Woolley
having been called on a mission to Colorado,
Millie P. Bassett was chosen to fill that
vacancy.
	As the ranks of the aids were reduced,
Olive Pixton was appointed on October 12,
1901 to act in that capacity. On May 3,
1902, Emma Jeremy and Lucy Chanberlain
were selected, and on May 2, 1903, Mary
T. Smith was appointed an aid, making a
complete band of noble, energetic workers
in the Primary cause.
	On April 11, 1903, Nellie Penrose Whit-
ney was appointed Stake Chorister, and
with her musical experience, combined
with the efficient service of Lizzie Thomas,
the musical department has been one of
the most pleasing features in the Primary
conferences and entertainments.
	In January, 1902, the Children’s Friend
the organ of the Primary Associations,
published by the General Board, was issued
and has since been a guide to the associa-
tion, lessons being prepared for each grade.
Since its introduction into the associations,
they have been conducted more uniformly,
and better work has been done.
	Since the reorganization of the Stake
Board in 1896, great improvement has
been made in Primary work in the Salt
Lake Stake of Zion. Through the untir-
ing efforts and wide experience of President
Comilla C. Cobb, and the aid and co-oper-
ation of her co-laborers, the associations
have increased in numbers and member- 
ship, and have been raised to a much
higher standard of excellence than form-
erly.
	Too much praise cannot be given to
President Cobb for her untiring zeal for the
advancement of the work, for her moth-
erly counsel and advice, and her loving

[Column 2]
words of encouragement, which will ever
be remembered by those who received them,
and will be a guide to those who may con-
tinue in the work.
	The Primary Stake Board has now dis-
solved on account of the division of the
Salt Lake Stake, but the members of the old
Board will always hold in affectionate re-
membrance the years they labored together
with love in their hearts for each other and
the children for whom they labored.
		ELLA W. HYDE, Stake Sec.


WOMAN'S EXPONENT.


ELIZABETH PIXTON.
	Sister Pixton, who was called suddenly
from this life, March 21, 1904, to join her
husband and loved ones who had gone be-
fore, had been for many years a conspicu-
ous figure in this vicinity; she was a wom-
an of great strength of character, and un-
common executive ability. She will be
missed in various ways, and in many
places. She was a woman of great integ-
rity to the Gospel and of a generous na-
ture, her good deeds will live in the mem-
ory of many whom she helped without
letting “her right hand know what the
left hand doeth,” according to the scrip-
ture.
	Sister Pixton was president of the Re-
lief Society of Taylorsville at the time of
her death, and had held the office over
twenty years.
	A sketch of her life was published in
the EXPONENT, February 15, 1900, short-
ly after she had celebrated her 80th birth-
day anniversary. On that happy occasion
about three hundred people assembled in
her honor at the meeting house in Tay-
lorsville. Many tributes were given her at
that time of affection and friendship, and
she was honored by all present each of the
speakers testifying to the high esteem in
which she was held by the community,
and her liberality to the poor and needy
was favorably commented upon.—E. B. W.
	The Relief Society officers of the Granite
Stake of Zion, to which she belonged,
wishing to do honor to her memory have
prepared the following—
RESOLUTIONS.
	Whereas our much esteemed sister and
colaborer, has by the hand of divine prov-
idence been removed from our midst, and
has, we feel confidently assured, gone to a
better world, we, her sisters, who know
of her loving deeds and appreciate her
many noble qualities, desire to express
our tender sympathy for the bereaved fam-
ily, and to acknowledge her labors and
ministrations of kindness, as a member of
the Relief Society, and also as a friend and
neighbor in the best sense of the word.
Therefore be it
	Resolved, we do humbly testify that
charity and humility were leading traits of
her character, and that her last act the day
before her death was one of charity, and
that her simple and unassuming manner
was beautifully illustrated in her last re-
quest, “that her coffin should be plain and
no flowers placed upon it, as the money
could be used to better advantage.”
	Resolved, that we tender our heartfelt
sympathy to the bereaved family of the de-
ceased and that they and the sisters of the
society over which she presided so long
may follow her noble example of diligence
and faithfulness.
	Resolved that these resolutions be re-


corded in our stake record and also pre-
sented to the family and published in the
WOMAN'S EXPONENT.
EMMA S. WOODRUFF,
MARY P. YOUNG,
CATHARINE H. BROCKBANK,
	In behalf of Granite Stake Relief Society.


RELIEF SOCIETY NURSE CLASS 1904-5.
	The above class will commence Septem-
ber 19th, 1904, continuing eight months.
Four lessons a week will be given by Dr.
Margaret C. Roberts, the class instructor,
and lectures by other physicians and sur-
geons of Salt Lake. There will also be a
course of lectures on cooking and ot her im-
portant subjects connected with the nurse
profession, by members of the General
Board of the Relief Society or by those
whom they may appoint.
	Charge of tuition $10 00.
	Students will furnish their own text
books designated by the Class Instructor.
	The students shall each sign a contract
to nurse for charity for seventy-five days or
whatever pay shall be designated by the
superintendent for two years from the date
of graduation.
	Anyone desiring to take the nurse course
and not paid for by the Relief Society of
her ward may do so by paying the ten dol-
lar fee herself and signing the contract.
All correspondence on this subject should
be addressed to Mrs. Phebe Y. Beatie, 55
N. West Temple Street.
				EMMA A. EMPEY,
	Superintendent Relief Society Nurses’
		Classes.


MOTHERS' WORK.
PAROWAN STAKE.
Lecture I.—Pre-existence of spirits.
	Sing Hymn, “O My Father.” Give a
talk explaining the doctrine expressed in
the hymn. Speaker read the hymn, as proof
that we have a Father in Heaven. Prove
that all existed with God and His only be-
gotten Son before we came to this earth.
What was our condition in our pre-exis-
tant state?
	References.—John, 1 14. John 16, 28.
John 17th, 5. Heb. 12, 9. Matt. 6. 9. Job
38, 4 7. Num. 27, 16. Ecc 12, 7. John 3,
2. Jer 1, 4-5. Rev. 12, 7-9. As set forth in
“Woman who art thou, What is thy Mis-
sion, By Pres. John Taylor. Added upon.
Jude 6.
	Questions.—1. What proofs have we
that we have a Father and Mother in
Heaven? Read explanation on marriage in
Compendium, also Gen. 1, 7. 2. Had we
developed as thoroughly as we could in the
spirit world? 3. Did we all keep our first
estate?
Lecture 2.—Object of existence.
	(a) Whence we came, (b) Who we are,
(c) Where are we going? (d) Object is to
benefit humanity and to gain eternal life.
	References.—(a) Pearl of Great Price,
page 32. Alma 13, 3-7. Eph. 1, 3-4-5.
Tit. 1, 2. (b) John 3, 2. Heb. 12, 9. (c)
I Nephi, 28, 8. Doc. and Cov. 43, 44-45.
Ecc. 12, 7. Pearl of Great Price, page 22.
Rev. 20, 6. Doc. and Cov. 29, II. (d)
Multiply and replenish the earth. Gen. 1,
28.
	Questions.—Will all get back to our
Father’s home? Doc. and Cov. 29, 9-14-21.
What was the first commandment given to
Adam and Eve? When was it given? How


[Column 1]
can we benefit humanity? How may we
gain eternal life.

Lecture 3.—Divinity of Parents Mission.

	Love and attributes as God planted them
in the human heart for a holy purpose. Mar-
riage a divine covenant for the propagation
of the race. Woman as a helpmate.
	References.—Read articles on marriage
(Com) John 5, 42. Tit. 3, 4. 1 Cor. 11, 11.
Doc. and Cov. Sec. 132. Sec. 49, 15-18.
1 Cor. 11, 3-12. Gen. 2, 24. Doc. and Cov.
Sec. 23. Gen. 2, 18.
	Questions.—Why is love implanted in
the human heart? 2. Is the mutual attrac-
tion of the sexes proper? 3 Enumerate the
ways in which women can be truly help-
mates. 4. When was woman divinely
chosen to be a mother? 5. What is truly
woman’s mission?

[-]ecture 4.

	(a) As mothers in Israel what manner
of women should we be? (b) Very prayer-
ful. (c) A sound mind in a sound body.
(d) Deportment to husband and children.
(e) Seek knowledge from all good sources,
truth, wisdom and the Holy Spirit. (f)
Motherhood a gift from God.
	References.—Doc and Cov. The prayer-
ful life by A. O. Woodruff, Young Wo-
man’s Journal Jan. 1[-]04. Eph. 5 chapter
22-25 vs.
	Questions.—1. What are some of the qual-
ifications necessary to be a true mother in
Israel? 2. Was the 25th Sec of the Doc.
and Cov. given for Emma Smith only? 3.
Should mothers devote their time entirely
to their home? 4. Why is it necessary to
keep our bodies sound and healthy? 5.
Why should we be careful in our deport-
ment around our home? 6. Is Motherhood
a gift from God?

Lecture 5—Prospective Mother.

	What shall we do to have our babies
healthy and happy? A life in harmony
with the gospel is the perfect example.
Discuss the physical needs of the body.
Freedom of dress, out door exercise, clean-
liness, food, fresh air and sunshine. Power
to guide and control oneself. Study well
one’s character before undertaking the grave
responsibility of Motherhood.
	Questions.—1. Give experiences what ef-
fect certain tendencies of the mother have
upon the child. 2. Does our temporal con-
dition effect our children? 3. To what ex-
tent should women exercise?

Lecture VI.

	Mental development, choice of literature.
The social life of the mother Spiritual de-
velopment, following the steps of the Sav-
ior. Parents may have the kind of spirits
they are worthy of.
	Questions.—1. What course should a
mother take in order that her child may
have the proper development? 2. What do
you think of mothers going into society
during this period? 3. Did the Roman and
Grecian nations honor prospective mothers?
4. Were children welcome by the Jewish
nation?

Lecture VII Care of the Infant.

	The baby’s wardrobe. How to care for
the infant. Discuss ventilation and sleep-
ing apartments. The baby’s food, when to
feed and quantity. Baby’s bath, baby’s ills
and how to treat them.
	Questions.—1. How should baby be

[Column 2]
dressed? 2. Why have flannel next to the
skin? Should the clothes be long or short?
3. Do you think a child should be fed at
regular intervals? 4. Why should a child
be bathed and how often? 5. Should a
baby learn to creep before it walks? 6.
Should a child be given water often? 7.
When the mother’s milk is insufficient,
what artificial food do you think best?

Lecture VIII.—First Years of the Child.

	Obedience is heavens first law. As the
twig is bent so the tree inclines. “Too
much indulgence has spoiled many a child,
too much love not one. Love brought the
child into the world and the more love we
give them the more we have for ourselves.”
“Love begets love.” Home is the child’s
world. Keep it uppermost in our
minds. One of the first steps is to gain
the child’s confidence. Habits are formed
in infancy.
	Questions.—What lessons of obedience
should a child learn in the first five years of
its life? 2 Illustrate love and indulgence.
3. In what way would we have more love
if we gave more? 4. Should we gratify the
child’s wishes about pleasure and outings?
5. How can we gain a child’s confidence?
6.What habits are formed in infancy?

Lecture IX.—Physical Development.

	Teach the child proper attitudes. Observ-
ance of the Word of Wisdom is essential to
physical development. The rights of chil-
dren in the home. Pay strict attention to
questions and give suitable answers. To
put children off or to send them away, what
effect does it have on the child’s character
	Questions.—1. What are proper attitudes.
2. How shall we entertain our children in
the home? 3. Should each child have some
part in keeping up the home? 4. What
effect has responsibility on character? 5.
How far should the freedom of a child ex-
tend in the home? 5. Why are idle mo-
ments dangerous to the child?

Lesson X.—Mental, Moral and Social
	Development.

	Overcome evil with good. Encourage
virtue and vice will disappear. Selection of
good books and companions should be
taught early in life. A mother’s influence.
The part a mother takes in the forming of
character cannot be over-estimated. It is a
social duty to be cleanly of speech, person
and dress.

	True politeness is to do and say,
	The kindest thing in the kindest way.

	Questions.—1. How can we overcome
evil with good? 2. Illustrate how we can
encourage virtue? 3. Name some good
books suitable for children of all ages. 4.
llustrate how a mother helps to form a
child’s character. 5. Why should we be
clean of speech, person and dress?

Lecture XI.—Spiritual Development.

	Each day should properly begin with
prayer. Peace, love and contentment come
from within. Christ the true ideal. One
life which embodies all the virtues, should
be made the example in the life of all. Give
special religious training in order that hon-
or for the priesthood may be fostered. Why
should the lesson of obedience be one of the
foundation stones of character building.
How is obedience connected with our spir-
tual progress?
	Questions.—1. Why should each day begin
with prayer? 2. Do favorable temporal sur-

[Column 3]
roundings make us contented and happy?
3. How do parents constantly teach morals
good and bad to their children? 4. How
soon does a child have a moral sense? 5.
What special religious training should be
given in order that children may obey or
honor the Priesthood?

Lecture XII.— The Development of the
	Child to Man and Womanhood.

	Teach the child the sacred laws of life be-
fore he gains the knowledge from evil
minds. Be sure you have his confidence
and then pay strict attention to every need.
Assist the children in choosing suitable
companions and books. During the devel-
opment period the boy and girl should be
tenderly and wisely guarded.
	Questions—Do you think children should
be taught the sacred laws of life, and how
soon? How can we gain their confidence,
and what are their needs? How can we as-
sist our children in choosing their compan-
ions and books? To what extent should
our boys and girls be allowed to participate
in dancing and other exciting pastime?

WOMAN’S EXPONENT.


WOMAN’S EXPONENT


IN MEMORIAM.
			MARY E. HARRIS.
 “Leaves have their time to fall, 
	And flowers to wither at the north wind’s breath, 
And stars to set—but all, 
Thou hast all seasons for thine own, oh Death.”
	Mary E. beloved wife of E. F. Harris, and
daughter of Thomas and Ann H. Gunn, departed
this life February 29, 1904, from pneumonia She 
was born September 15, 1868, in Adamsville,
Beaver County. She was a counselor in the Y. 
L. N. M. I. Association, and secretary of the Sab-
bath School in that ward for a number of years. 
She taught school in every settlement of Beaver 
County except one. She was married in Septem-
ber, 1891, to Brother C. J. Harris, of Beaver City,
who in the following October took his departure to


fill a mission to Great Britain. During his ab
sence she supported herself by teaching school and
also contributed towards his support while in the
mission field. She was the mother of three sons,
one ef whom preceded her to the spirit world. She
filled the office of stake secretary of the Relief
Society in an able and satisfactory manner for a
year and a half previous to her demise Although
she had been a partial invilid for several years,
she was a devoted and affectionate wife and
mother, and was an efficient Latter-day Saint in
every sense of the word. She leaves a grief-
stricken husband, two children, an aged mother,
one brother, four sisters and a host of relatives and
friends to mourn her untimely departure.
	Funeral services were held in the L D. S.
meeting house November 2, 1903, at 2 o’clock p.
m. The speakers all paid a high tribute to her
pure, exemplary life and stirling worth. She was
highly respected, esteemed and beloved by all
who knew her.
		RESOLUTIONS OF RESPECT.
	Whereas an Alwise Creator has seen fit to call
from this sphere of action our worthy and beloved
sister and co-laborer, Mary E. Harris, therefore
be it
	Resolved, that we strive to emulate her unwav-
ering faith, her fidelity to purity and truth, her de-
votion to her home and religious duties, and her
humility and respect and obedience to proper
authority.
	Resolved that we extend our heartfelt sympathy
and love to the bereft husband and children and
to the worthy mother and to the brothers and sis-
ters and to all other dear ones and that we sin-
cerely pray our Heavenly Father that He will fill
their wounded souls with the comforting and heal-
ing influence of His Holy Spirit, that they may
realize that she has only gone before to enjoy the
rest and reward of a well spent life.
	That a copy of these resolutions be given to the
esreemed husband and the worthy mother, and
one be sent to the Woman’s Exponent for publi-
cation.
			ALICF M. T. TANNER, Sec.
Beaver, Utah, March 10, 1904.


RESOLUTIONS OF RESPECT.
	In Memory of Sister Josephine F. Nash who de-
parted this life, July 25, 1903, at Salem, Utah,
was born in Letmis Tiania, Norway, Feb. 23, 1854.
Adopted by the Relief Society.
	Whereas, our Heavenly Father, in his infinite
wisdom has seen fit to call home our beloved sister
and
	Whereas, we feel in the death of Sister Nash a


sore bereavement and regret that she should be
called from our midst so soon, be it
	Resolved, that we do express our sorrow and ex-
tend our sympathy to her family, and also, be it
	Resolved, that we keep in memory her great and
noble patience, and loving kindness to all fellow
mortals and that we ever emulate her example.
	Resolved that these resolutions be published in
the WOMAN’S EXPONENT and that a copy be given
to the family of Sister Nash.
						SISTER GARDNER.
	In Memory of Sister Annie Peterson, Born in
Plesing, Denmark, Nov. 20, 1814. Died in Salem,
Utah, Jan. 15, 1904. Adopted by Relief Society.
	Whereas, our beloved sister Annie Peterson,
has after a long and useful life, been called home
by our Heavenly Father, be it
	Resolved, that we express our love for sister
Peterson and our appreciation for the good and
noble work she has done among us, Be it also
	Resolved, that we sympathize with her beloved
ones who still remain with us and who feel the loss
of a mother and wise counselor.
	Resolved, that these resolutions be published in
the WOMAN’S EXPONENT, and that a copy of them
be sent to her family.
						MARY EDMAN.


INTERNATIONAL QUINQUENNIAL.
INFORMATION CONCERNING THE AP-
PROACHING THIRD QUINQUENNIAL OF
THE INTERNATIONAL COUNCIL AND THE
INTERNATIONAL CONGRESS IN BERLIN.
	I am in constant receipt of letters asking
for more minute information than has been
given in previous articles in regard to var-
ious details of the approaching Quinquen-
nial and its accompanying international Con-
gress. Every inquirer has received a per-
sonal reply, but, as the plans are matured,
and in that process necessarily to some ex-
tent modified from time to time, I will ask
all interested in the subjects to consider that
this constitutes a correct answer to date to
any questions that may have been asked
me. If, as in some instances will be the
case, the information communicated differs
from that communicated in personal letters,
I beg them to understand that the differ-
ence in the dates between this communica-
tion and the letters they may have receivd
from my office, accounts for any changes
in the information.
	Being in receipt of frequent advices from
Berlin I can confidently assure my country-
women that every provision possible is be-
ing made by the German committee of Ar-
rangements for the succees of both Council
and Congress, and for the comfort, conveni
ence and hospitable entertainment of all
who may attend them. The German Com-
mittee of Arrangements is responsible for
the preparations for the Congress only,
which is to be held under the auspices of
the International Council and immediately
succeeding the Quinquennial proper; but
the German Committee is interpreting its
responsibilities very generously. Although
the preparations for the Council proper are
in the hands of a large Committee of Ar-
rangements composed of the General officers
of the International, the Presidents of all
affiliated National Councils, and one mem-
ber from each Council especialy appointed
to serve on this committee, and, although
this committee of which the President of
the International Council is the Chairman,
has worked constantly since Mid September
for the success of the Council, it goes with-
out saying that there are many matters of
detail relating to the local situation which
cannot be arranged by correspondence. In
regard to all these, the German Committee
is generous with both advice and practical
help.
	It is not improbable that all the sessions


[Column 1]
of the Council, Executive and Public, may
be held in the Reichstag Building. This
is greatly desired, but if the strenuous eff-
orts to secure it fail, beautiful and ample
accommodations will be found in the Phil-
harmonie. Should the Council be honored
by being granted the Reichstag, as even its
large hall will be too small to accommodate
the Council on the occasion of its Peace and
Arbitration meeting, and, as for other reas-
sons, the Parliament House will not be suit-
able for such a meeting, that may probably
be held in the very beautiful Cirkus Schu-
man. Either in the Reichstag or in the
Philharmonie, a room will be set aside for
the use of the Council during the Quinquen-
nial week as a Central Bureau, or what in
this country we should call a “Business
Headquarters.” The Chairman of the
Sub-Committee on Hospitality recommends
as what we in the United States would call
“Hotel Headquarters” Das Palast Hotel,
Koenig Gratzer Strasse, Leipziger Platz.
	This hotel is conveniently situated to the
halls in which the meetings of both Coun-
cil and Congress will be held, and is rec-
commended as being in all respects desir-
able.
	The cost per day of a room with light
and service included is from four to ten
marks according to location; of breakfast
one mark and a half. I am at the present
time in correspondence with the proprietor
of the hotel and hope to secure such accom-
modations as will enable us to announce as
Hotel and Social Headquarters. As one of
the chief advantages of such a gathering of
women from all parts of the world is the
promotion of their real acquaintance with
one another, it seems to me most desirable
that as many as possible shall secure their
rooms in this hotel recommended as head-
quarters. If any of our American women
going over desire a cheaper hotel, one in
the same neighborhood named “Hotel
Bellevue” in Potsdamer Platz is recom-
mended. Here according to location rooms
are from three to seven marks, breakfast
one and one fourth marks, luncheon and
dinner in proportion. For the benefit of
any Council of women who prefer to make
other arrangements, I give the address of
the chairman of that special section of the
Hospitality Committee whose functions it
is to find accommodations in hotels and
pensions, and will remind ladies who prefer
privite hospitality that the German women
have very generously provided to secure
this in so far as possible for those applying
for it. Frau Cubitz, Lutzow Str. 85 B. Ber-
lin W. Germany.
	I am in constant receipt of letters from
women who have received the general invi-
tation to speak at the Congress or to “par-
ticipate” in it, asking for a statement of
their specific subjects, and of details con-
cerning their individual work. Since it is
only with the details of the Council pro-
gram that I am directly and responsibly
concerned, I am obliged to refer all such to
the chairman of the Committee of Arrange-
ments, Frau Marie Strit, Wintergarten,
Strasse 3, Dresden, Germany, or to the Cor-
responding Secretary of the German Coun-

[Column 2]
cil whose address is Berlin West 10 Frieder-
ich Wilhelm Strasse 7.
	As for the program of the Council proper,
it is being developed, with as much rapidity
as could be expected where so many coun-
tries are involved. The President has is-
sued a call to the Committee of Arrange-
ments inviting as many members as possi-
ble to convene in a preliminary meeting on
Saturday, June 4th, so that any inside de-
tails, of which there are always so many on
the eve of any great gathering, may be
provided for. It must be understood that
all preparation that can possibly be made
by correspondence, will be made in ad-
vance; that the President of the Interna-
tional Council will arrive in Berlin a week
or ten days before the opening of the
Quinquennial, and that the preparations
which must be made on and after June 4th
will be reduced to a minimum. The pro-
gram for the Quinquennial in large is as
follows:—
		SATURDAY, JUNE 4TH.
	Meeting of the Committee of Arrange-
ments to endorse arrangements already
made by the Executive in Dresden supple-
mented by correspondence during the year,
etc., etc., and to take action upon final de-
tails.
		MONDAY, JUNE 6TH.
	The morning will be occupied by meet
ings of Standing and Special Committees of
which there are now five.
	a) Press, whose chairman is Mrs. Emily
Cummings, 44 Dewson St., Toronto, Canada.
	b) Peace and Arbitration, whose acting
chairman is Mrs. May Wright Sewall, 633
N. Penna. St. Indianapolis, Ind.
	c) Legal Position of Women, whose chair-
man is Baroness Olga von Beschwitz. 5
Mozart Str. Dresden Germany.
	d) Finance, whose chairman is Mrs Dig-
ham 284 St. George St. Toronto, Canada.
	e) International Relations whose acting
chairman is the President.
	The President has asked the chairman of
every committee to convene her associates
at this time in order that any propositions
which the different committees may wish to
submit to the Executive may be considered
in such special sessions of the committees.
	The afternoon of Monday will be occu-
pied by a meeting of the Executive Comm-
ittee proper when the program will in-
clude:—
	a) To receive reports of Standing and
Special Committees.
	b) To affiliate New Councils.
	c) To elect New Patrons.
	d) To present unfinished business which
has been turned over to this particular
session.
		TUESDAY, JUNE 7TH.
	Both morning and afternoon will be occu-
pied by sessions of the Executive.
	The mornings of Wednesday, Thursday,
and Friday, June 8th, 9th and 10th will
also be occupied by sessions of the Execu-
tive before which many important matters
announced in the Agenda will be brought.

[Column 3]
	All the numerous amendments to the
constitution which have been proposed dur-
ing the Quinquennial period will be brought
forward for action. While many of these
are merely formal, and others concern
merely the method of transacting business,
there are still a large number of vital im-
portance. It will be proposed at the ap-
proaching Quinquennial that two very im-
portant subjects be added to the one which
has already in 1899 been adopted for propa-
ganda. Thus far, the Council has been
committed to making propaganda only for
Peace and Arbitration. At Berlin, it will
consider the propriety of adding to its pro-
gram.
	a) Abolition of White Slave Traffic.
	b) Political Equality for Women.
	I wish to call attention to the phrasing
of this second proposition. Even our
Council Workers often speak of this as being
a recommendation of “Suffrage for Women”
and then treat this phrase as if “Suffrage
for Women” means the same degree of
political liberty in all countries. A little
reflection will show that between “Woman’s
Suffrage” as it is understood in the United
States and “Political Equality for Women”
as it is understood in the Council, there is
a wide difference, but the principle demon-
strated in the former is clearly recognized
in the latter. The proposition as it will
stand on the Agenda is as follows:—
	“INASMUCH AS ALL GOVERN-
	MENTS EQUALLY EFFECT THE
	MEN AND WOMEN LIVING UNDER
	THEM. THEREFORE, BE IT RE-
	SOLVED THAT UNDER ALL GOV-
	ERNMENTS WHETHER NOMINALLY
	REPUBLICAN OR MONARCHICAL
	WHATEVER POLITICAL RIGHTS OR
	PRIVILEGES ARE ACCORDED TO
	MEN OUGHT ON CORRESPONDING
	CONDITIONS TO BE ACCORDED TO
	WOMEN.”
	It will be seen that this proposition seeks
to abolish the political distinctions which
have been based solely on sex.
		[TO BE CONTINUED.]


IN MEMORIAM
	Died at Lewiston, Cache county, Utah, March
7, 1904, Sister Abigail Thorne Pond, one of Utah’s
Pioneers Sister Pond was born in the town of
Sempronious, now Moravia, Cayuga county, state
of New York, April 2, 1821. being at time of her
death 82 years, 11 months and 5 days old. She
embraced the Gospel in her childhood, being bap-
tized on the 30th of September, 1838, and was the
first in the neighborhood where she lived to offer
herself for this sacred ordinance. She emigrated
to Nauvoo in 1841, and was well acquainted with
the Prophet and Patriarch Joseph and Hyrum
Smith. She was taught the principle of plural
marriage and embraced the same, being married
in the Nauvoo Temple Jan. 26, 1846, to Elder
Samuel Russel. They left Nauvoo with the
Saints when they were driven from their homes,
and endured cheerfully all the hardships and per-
secutions of that historic period. They arrived in
Salt Lake City, Utah, in September, 1841, Some
time after their arrival in Utah the husband apos-
tatized and went with his first family to California.
Some years later she became the wife of Brother
Stillman Pond.
	Brother and Sister Pond were among the early
settlers of Richmond, where they raised their fam-
ily and where Brother Pond went to his well-
earned rest. Sister Pond then moved to Lewiston,
where most of her children were living, and has
resided here for a number of years, tenderly loved
by her children, grand-children, and a host of
friends. She was a faithful Latter-day Saint, and
a devoted wife, a kind and loving mother, and a
friend to the oppressed. She was a member of
the Relief Society in Nauvoo, was president of the
organization in Richmond for a number of years,
and was a faithful worker until her health failed.
She awaits a glorious resurrection with the just.
RHODA M. HENDRICKS,
Stake Secretary.

THE HEALER
Small bud with its frail stem broken,
Sweet song that was left unsung—
Fond love that was never spoken—
And the harp that was left unstrung!

Fair bud with its frail stem bended,
Bruised flower that might have bloomed,
All future promises ended—
All of thy glories entombed,

Ah! the harp sent forth no message,
Alas! it was left unstrung—
Its echoes all hushed and silent!
The beautiful song unsung,

Love-words, which never were spokeu,
Left longings forever void—
The vows which were made and broken—
Bright visions and hopes destroyed.

Then where, oh, where is the guerdon
For hopes all blighted so soon?
The potent touch of the Master,
Will set life’s music in tune.
ELLIS R. SHIPP.


A STORM ON THE ATLANTIC OCEAN.

	The storm-king reigns in fury,
		The sea is dashing high;
	The waves white capped are lashing,
		Where e’er may rest the eye.
	Our steamer good the “Cretic”
		Rides o’er the billowy wave,
	Then sinks deep in a valley
		As though in wat’ry grave.

	The storm-king’s legions howling,
		Incessant chorus sing;
	On masts and ropes are playing
		Wild winds, whose tones they fling.
	O, grand the storm-king’s fury,
		The tempest’s awful sound;
	When as abyss, then seething
		The waters rush around.

	The sky of inky blackness
		With ne’er a ray serene,
	Envelopes as a mantle,
		Rain fierce beats in between;
	A sea comes o’er the vessel,
		With crushing, deaf’ning roar;
	Receding, then is sullen;
		O, may it come no more.


’Mid creaking of the timbers
		Propeller’s whirl in air,
	And madd’ning splash of water,
		I hear my loved ones’ prayer,
	Far o’er the Rocky mountains,
		I know they plead for me, 
	That safe we’ll reach the harbor, 
		Across the stormy sea.
					LYDIA D. ALDER.


[Column 1]
DISCIPLINE AND DEVELOPMENT.
	“Obedience to parental command was
Heaven’s first law”. It is no less important
now than it was at the beginning of man’s
career on the earth.	Happy indeed is the
home where a proper discipline is main-
tained; with Paul we say “Children obey
your parents in the Lord:” for it is right.
“Honor thy father and mother;” which is
the first commandment with promise that it
may be well with thee and thou mayest live
long on the earth. And ye “fathers, provoke
not your children to wrath, but bring them
up in the nurture and admonition of the
Lord.” I consider the foregoing very ap-
propriate in the lesson of mother’s work.
Parents should exercise proper control over
the child.	And in order that we mothers
may find out better the way to properly
control our children, I wil endeavor to bring
out a few points. Why should the parent
control the child? Why not let him follow
his own inclination? I think we can all
readily see what kind of a household that
would be; instead of the home being one of
peace and order that it should be, just the
opposite would be the result, Secondly:
that proper ends may be accomplished with
the least effort. The third reason, for the
sake of character development, which is a
very good reason; for if a child is left to
grow up in this world where there is so
much sin without some one to watch and
guard him and point out to him both the
good and the evil, and whenever the least
tendency toward evil is shown, to check it
at once; he is apt to develop much more of
the evil than the good. 
	One of the first lessons to the child should
be that of prompt and willing obedience to
parents. And the very best way to obtain
this result, is never to require or ask a
child to do anything that is beyond its cap
acity; and we should consider all the cir-
cumstances surrounding it. And then after
the requirement is made, always see to it
that the child obeys the request. Now in
this connection a great deal depends upon
how we speak to a child when we ask him
to do anything. If we speak in a command-
ing or harsh tone of voice, and at the same
time call him lazy or something similar it
is no more than natural that the child rebels
and refuses to obey. While on the other
hand if he is spoken to in a gentle tone of
voice, and at the same time calling him by
some pet name, he will almost invariably
jump up and run to do the mother’s bid-
ding. And let me say to you mothers,when
your child does this, never forget to repay
him with a smile, a gentle caress and a
pleasant thank you.

[Column 2]
	Parents should get the child’s confidence.
This is very true, and a very improtant fac-
tor in helping to guide the child right.
Mothers should encourage their children in
telling about the little incidents that hap-
pen to them when out of her company.	I
have seen mothers when their little boy or
girl has come full of excitement about
something that has happened; instead of
that mother listening and taking a lively
interest in both the joys and sorrows of what
the child has to relate, she will possibly give
him or her a push, and say “Oh dont both-
er me, I dont care anything about that.” A
child treated in this way a few times soon
becomes reticent and don’t bother to tell
mother of the little troubles of everyday life,
and in that way the mother loses her op-
portunity of giving her counsel when it
would be of the most benefit. And as the
girl and boy grow older instead of making
mother the confidential friend and advisor
that she should be, their confidence is placed
somewhere else, and in some instances the
counsel they receive from others, instead of
leading them right has just the opposite
effect.
	There is another view of thls subject. A
parent’s every action should be such that
the children will have the utmost cofid-
dence in them. I consider the practice that
some parents indulge in of deceiving their
children and making promises to them that
they never intend to fulfill the worst kind of
discipline. Parents should be very careful
about making promises to their children;
they should consider all the circumstances;
and then, after the promise is given, always
see that it is fulfilled: unless it is impossible
for reasons that the child himself can under-
stand. While on the other hand a parent
that is continually making and breaking
promises soon loses the confidence of the
children and as a consequence the power of
control is weakened. In a family we find
different characteristics in each child and
for that reason no special rule can be laid
down; different methods may be necessary
for the training of each child. Therefore,
how necessary that parents should do all in
their power to gain that knowledge necess-
ary to rear their children, so that they may
become noble men and women. And how
thankful we should be that we are so situa-
ted that we can avail ourselves of good
teachers for our children. Our brethren saw
the necessity of this; therefore the different
organizations in the church for the benefit
of the young. All churches have the Sunday
School but that is not sufficient so in ad-
dition to our Sunday School we have the
Primary and Religion classes and Improve-
ment Associations. If a child is sent regu-
larly to these different organizations as they
get old enough, and they learn the lessons
there assigned to them, there is not much
danger of their going very wrong.	But
some parents say I can’t get my children to
go, they will go to Sunday School occasion-
ally and quit that altogether when they are
sixteen or thereabouts. What’s the matter?
well there are different reasons; but do you
know that I think that the greatest fault lies
with the parents. Sometimes they are kept


[Column 1]
at home for some trifling thing, and they
lose their interest; and sometimes they
have lessons assigned them, and instead of
the mother helping her boy or girl in
preparing that lesson If it should be a little
difficult, they will perhaps not give them
the necessary time in which to prepare the
same. And as we all know it sometimes
takes a very little thing to discourage a
child. But if they once get out of the habit
of going, Oh, how hard it is to get them
started again. So for that reason, we should
be on guard and when any inclination is
shown of not wanting to attend meetings,
use every endeavor to overcome their objec-
tions. I dont believe in forcing a boy or
girl to meeting or threatening them with
punishment if they don’t go, but kindly
show them the good that they will derive
from attending. Now while we are on the
subject of good teachers, I would like to say
a few words in favor of sending our boys
and girls to the Church schools, for I know
as well as I want to, that it is one of the
greatest of all helps to parents in making
good Latter-day Saints of their children.
When boys and girls are about sixteen or
eighteen we all know that it is about the
hardest time for the parents to get them to
attend to their religious duties; but if we
place them in a Church school about that
time and keep them there for a few years,
and at the same time give them the proper
example and training at home, I think that-
in ninety cases out of a hundred they will
come out good Latter-day Saints, besides
being prepared in other ways for the battle
of life.
	Parents should be united in the govern-
ment of the child. I think we will all agree
on that, for it would certainly be an uphill
business in training any child where the
parents were not united in their methods of
government. One parent should never in-
terfere with the other while the child is be-
ing corrected or punished, but if you feel
that the correction or punishment is unjust,
wait until the child is out of hearing before
finding fault in any way; and then try to
harmonize ideas for the benefit of the child.
Parents that have the habit of finding fault
with each other before their children cannot
expect anything else but that they will
grow up quarrelsome with each other and
their playmates; and not only that, but they
lose the reverence and respect for their par-
ents that they should have. Another reas-
on why one parent should not interfere with
the other, is that it is apt to make the child
feel that he is being unjustly dealt with.
	The next question, what condition of
mind should the parent be in while admin-
stering punishment? The answer should
be, never punish in anger. I wonder how
many there are of us that follow that rule,
I dare say that none of us do it altogether.
But it is a true principle nevertheless.
Some of the best thinkers and writers tell
us that we should never whip a child while
angry. Now I should like to know how
many mothers would whip unless they were
angry. I think there are very few, but we
don’t like to acknowledge that we whipped
our child because we were angry. Oh no,
we say, it was because he had done some-
thing for which he deserved a thrashing.
But I would like to ask; does whipping do
any good anyway? We can all answer that
for ourselves, but I am decidedly of opinion
that it does more harm than good every time.
Then you might say, what are you going
to do to correct your child when he does

[Column 2]
wrong?, the answer is this. The home
should be as far as possible, natural, and of
such a nature, that the child himself will
feel that the correction is just. For instance,
if a child tells a falsehood, and it becomes
known to the rest of the family, he should
be made to feel that he has done that for
which he deserves the suspicion of the whole
family. All eyes shou'd be turned upon
him with a pitying distrust. Or if your little
boy whittles your furniture with his new
pocket-knife and you give him a slap for it
perhaps as soon as your back is turned he
will only do it again. But if you deprive him
of the use of his knife for a few days, he
will have time to realize the loss of it, and
he will not be very likely to repeat the
offense.
	I don’t think that any good ever came
from telling a little child; “The bad man
will get you if you don’t be good" or that
“There’s a big bear out there” or similar
expressions. In fact very bad effects follow
such practices. In the first place the child
is frightened, and is apt to grow to be of a
nervous disposition; but that is not the worst
of it. It will not be long before the child
finds out that he is being deceived, that the
mother to whom he has always looked as
being perfect has actually been telling a
falsehood. Mothers did you ever think of
it in that way before? did you ever stop to
realize that you were teaching your little
child the first lesson in deception. It is just
such practices and others that I have tried,
in this lecture to show the evil effects of, that
tend to weaken parental control. I have
already given you my opinion of whipping
children as a means of punishment and as
I said before, it not only does no good
but causes a feeling of resentment, and has
often been the means of causing children to
tell falsehoods; and what more could we ex-
pect than that a child who gets a whipping
every time it does wrong; naturally tries to
evade that kind of punishment, even if he
has to tell a falsehood to do so.
	I have [-]ndeavored in my weak way to
follow the subject of this lecture; and to
sum it all up. we find that the main points
to be remembered are. Unity of parents.
Kindness but not indulgence. Firmness but
not harshness. And above all let the ex-
ample be such that when our everyday life
is reflected in the actions of our children,
that they may bring joy and happiness to
us instead of sorrow and remorse. May
this be our happy lot is the sincere desire of
ELIZABETH FLETCHER, PROVO.

		MOTHER’S WORK.
		SNOWFLAKE STAKE.

Lesson VII.—Employment for Children.

	“From toil he wins his spirits light
	From busy day the peaceful might.”
								GRAY.
	(a) A busy life cannot well be otherwise
than cheerful, (b) Let the child assist his
parents in the home at a very early age and
help him to feel that his aid is necessary
and appreciated, (c) Cultivate a desire in
the child to work his way honestly and
bravely, (e) All labor that is honest is
honorable too. (f) Parents do wrong in
keeping their children too much sheltered
and enervated by parental indulgence.

Lesson VIII.—Amusements.

	(a) Have them adapted to both the ment-

[Column 2]
al and physical development of the child,
(b) Teach him to invent games and be will-
ing for him to invite other children in to
participate. (c) Do not let amusements in-
fringe upon the sacredness of the Sabbath
Day or upon the rights and property of
others, (d) Avoid excesses particularly in
dancing (Ref. Juvenile Instructor page 246,
Vol 37.

Lesson IX.—Politeness

	“Politeness is to do and say
	The kindest thing in the kindest way.”
	(a) Proper respect for the rights and
privileges of one another at home and
abroad. (b) Politeness is the result of good
sense and good nature. It is a pait of good
morals. (c) Levity of manner unbecoming,
(d) Americans are afflicted with pretension.
Let the Latter-day Saint mother seek to
eradicate the evil. (e) “A graceful be-
havior towards superiors, inferiors and
equals is a constant source of pleasure.”

Lesson X.—Make Home Attractive.

		“Let Love Abound,”
	(a) A gilded palace is not a home, it is
not a formal school of staid solemnity and
rigid discipline that constitutes a home. (b)
“’Tis there the twig is bent.” (c) Encour-
age music both vocal and instrumental. It
induces children to spend their evenings at
home. (d) Pictures of a proper character
have a refining influence. (e) Flower gar-
dens cultivated by both boys and girls.

Lesson XI.—Make Home Attractive. (con-
tinued).

	“You may break, you may shatter the vase if you
		will.
	But the scent of the roses will hang’round it still.”
	(a) Family devotions. No child can fail
to be impressed where all is sincerity. (b)
Good books are as necessary as good com-
pany. They are an evidence of culture. Let
them be adapted to the capacity of the
child. (c) Make a specialty of church
works. (d) Baneful effects of dime novels.
(Ref. Juvenile Instructor page 274-586,
Vol. 37).

Lesson XII.—Habits of Children

	“Habit in a child is at first like a spider web if
neglected it becomes a thread of twine next a cord
of rope finally a cable—then who can break it?”
	(a) Habit is a man’s best friend or worst
enemy. (b) All the virtues or vices, all the
actions of mind or body may be chained by
its despotic power. (c) The importance of
forming only good habits. (d) Bad habits
are broken only by perseverance and earnest
prayer. (e) The importance of mothers
watching the habits of her children from
earliest infancy, (e) Regularity in diet,
sleep, work, play etc.
	Emma S Smith, President; Sarah Driggs,
Mary J. K. West, Counselors; Della F.
Smith, Secretary; Janet M. Smith Treas-
urer.


Fort Madison, Ia., boasts having as one of
its most respected and patriotic citizens
Mrs. Rachel Albright, who is a grand-
daughter of Betsy Ross, who made the
first American flag. Mrs. Albright was
born in the home of her grandmother,
Betsy Ross, in Philadelphia, June 16, 1812
and was brought up under Mrs. Ross’ care.
She there learned the art of making flags
at her grandmother’s knee, and Mrs. Al-
bright still carries on this work.

MY TREASURES.
		BY RAY HULBERT.
I am standing today on the evergreen bank
	O’erhanging the River of Time;
In the distance behind me are memories rank—
	Before me the future sublime.

About me are twining the affections of old,
	Like tendrils of new-grown moss;
While pictures of silver and threads of gold
	Are gleaming amid flakes of floss.

As I stand on the banks of this beautiful shore,
	Now washed by the Waters of Life,
I find fresh among the bright treasures of yore,
	The pearls of the present are rife.

And my heart wells upward, toward the Great
		White Throne,
	As I glean of these rubies so bright,
And I offer to him who sitteth enthroned,
	My treasures for His World of Light.


VISIT IN ST. GEORGE STAKE.
	The Presidency of the St. George Stake
Relief Society left St. George May 2nd, and
in the afternoon met with the Relief Society
of Leeds where Bishop McMullin met with
us and we reorganized the Society.
	Sister Susan Harris, the former president,
has moved to Wyoming, and Helen Mc-
Mullin takes her place as president. Ada
McMullin and Mary Olsen counselors. The
sisters were well pleased with our visit.
Sister Mary G. White, Stake president of
the Primary was with us and the Primary
was also reorganized. Caroline McMullin
president, Mrs. Frank McMullin and Emma
Angel counselors; we had a nice meeting,
and went on to Toquerville where counse-
lor Mary D. Batty presided at meeting of
the Relief Society as president Spilsbury
could not attend. There was a full attend-
ance and the organization was complete.
The next morning at 8 a. m. held primary
and just before the meeting closed the
school bell rang and the children ran from
the meeting like a flock of sheep, for the
school room. We then went to Virgen by
the “twist” which is a hard road, and held
Relief Society at 2 p. m. with President
Hilton presiding. We had an enjoyable
time. The sisters appreciate the yearly
visits. We here appointed Primary meet-
ings to be held as we returned.
	We went on to Grafton where we had a
general meeting. So few people live there
that it is hard to keep up the associations,
but they are striving to do right. We spent
the night with Lottie Russell and others.
Went to Rockville on the 8th and had
meeting at 10 o’clock Mary L. Stout presid-
ing: she is the mother of 15 children and
quite a young looking woman, another lady
there has had the same number. “Utah’s
best crop,” is exhibited here. We met with
77 children in the Primary association Sis-
ter Farnes presiding.
	At the close of the meeting we went on
to Springdale and spent the night at the
Bishop’s. The scenery here is grand and the
fresh mountain air is very invigorating.
	At 10 o’clock a. m. met with the Relief 
Society, Bishop Oliver Gifford met with
us. At 2 p. m. met with Primary and re-
turned to Rockville, where we spent the
night with Sister Ida Miller a widow. 
	We went to Virgen on the 6th and met
with the Primary, many of the mothers met
with us, Sister Stratton presided, we had a
nice meeting and returned to Toquerville,
and held an officers meeting with the Relief
Society.


Went to Leeds on the 7th and had Pri-
mary meeting, Caroline McMullin presiding,
a lot of well behaved children live here.
We went on to Harrisburg and met with the
people there, had a good time.
	We returned to St. George having been
gone 6 days, held 13 meetings, traveled 90
miles and enjoyed ourselves very much,
visiting with the Saints.
				ANN C. WOODBURY


HOME AND ITS INFLUENCE.
	If we only realized the importance the
influence of home has upon the life of our
children, we would strive to make it the
most desirable place for them.
	This can be done by making the children
feel that it is necessary for them to do their
part to bring this about.
	Children ought to be taught to be help-
ful and considerate for their parents or
elders. Mothers make a great mistake when
they bring their children up to expect to be
waited upon.
	It naturally makes them selfish, when if
they are taught from their infancy to per-
form small acts to save mother, and if it is
impressed upon their minds through love,
it will come quite natural to them to do so.
Many mothers may think that this is too
much trouble, they would rather do it them-
selves it is too much bother; we must not
think this if we wish them to grow up to be
useful; we wish them to be happy and have
a good time and therefore they should be
helpful.
	The influence of home and its surround-
ings stay by us all through our lives, then
how careful we ought to be that those im-
pressions are of a pleasant and profitable
nature.
	We should be very careful of the com-
pany that they keep; rather have them
bring company home, than go out to seek
it, by doing so we have a better chance to
judge of their companions.
	It is also necessary that we as mothers
set before them by example as well as pre-
cept that we should wish them to follow
“as we sow so shall we reap,” if we make
good impressions they will be lasting.
	Solomon says, “My son keep thy fathers
commandment and forsake not the law of
thy mother. Bind them continually npon
thy heart and tie them about thy neck.”
	“When thou goest, it shall lead thee; and
when thou sleepest, it shall keep thee; and
when thou awakest it shall talk with thee.”
	The wise man Solomon realized this.
MARY A. C. LAMBERT.


MOTHERS’ WORK.
SOUTH SANPETE STAKE.
	Pre-natal life and hints to prospective
mothers.

	Lecture I.— The first great command, “Mul-
tiply and replenish the earth”
	(a) Necessity of faith in God as a guiding
star to expectant mother. (b) Urgent ne-
cessity for a knowledge of the sin and
danger of destroying pre-natal life. (c)
Danger of marking children greatly exag-
gerated, mothers should not permit their
thoughts to dwell on unpleasant and disa-
greeable things. (d) Duty of mothers to cul-


tivate the qualities they wish their children
to possess.

Lecture II.

	(a) Necessity of society, one’s proper com-
panionship for pregnant women. (b) Pro-
priety of going out during this period. (c)
Folly of false modesty. (d) Dress hygienic
and becoming. (e) No corsets or tight
bands. (f) Clothing suspended from the
shoulders. (g) Rubbing with oil to relieve
tension of abdominal muscles.

Lecture III.

	(a) Abundant exercise. (b) Daily walks.
(c) How to avoid constipation by correct
diet. (d) Plenty of fresh fruit and pure
water. (e) Abstain from tea, coffee, and
all drugs and stimulants, live according to
nature.

Lecture IV.—Heredity.

	(a) Grave responsibilities of parents. (b)
Injustice to children who are forced to in-
herit disease and sin. (c) Necessity of a
prayerful united effort on the part of parents
to overcome tendencies, whether physical,
moral or mental, that they may not be trans-
mitted to their children. (d) Rights of
children to be well born. 

Lecture V.—Care of Mother after confine-
ment.

	(a) She should insist on perfect ventilation
and scrupulous cleanliness. (b) Also abso-
lute quiet and freedom from care. (c) Mild
and simple diet of such a nature as will pre-
vent constipation. (d) Tea and coffee are
harmful in their effects. (e) No stimulants
unless ordered by a competent physician.
(e) No callers should be permitted.

Lecture VI.—Care of the Infant.

	(a) No water for the first bath. (b)
Thorough rubbing with oil applied with a
soft cloth followed by careful wiping. (c)
No food except the contents of the mother’s
breast and warm water which has been
previously boiled. (d) Don’t waken a child
to exhibit to visitors. (e) Avoid strong light,
tight bands and too much clothing. (f)
Also clothes unnecessarily long. (g) Give
frequent and thorough rubbing with the
hand, (h) Regular nursing is important.
	ALVIRA L. COX, President R.S.


The U. S. D. R. met May 16 at the home
of Mrs. George A. Smith. The rooms
were elaborately decorated with the Stars
and Stripes, Society colors, pictures and
the flags of nearly all the European coun-
tries. Regent Mrs. Isabel M. Whitney
Sears presided and after the usual prelimin-
aries Mrs. Sears read an entertaining sketch
of the life of Nathan Hale of revolutionary
fame. Mrs. Philo T. Farnsworth followed
with a poem to his memory written by a
daughter of the Revolution Virginia Frayer
Bayle, Miss Wells gave the courtship of
this celebrated hero. There were present
as guests Mrs. Charlotte F. Richards, Mrs.
Lucy Smith Acomb and Mrs. Charles W.
Nibley. The meeting was very enjoyable
and after adjournment delicious refresh-
ments were served to all in a dainty fashion
and a social hour was made particularly
pleasant.
